Survey: Baptist among Florida's best hospitals;
Two Baptist locations also rated best among First Coast facilities

BYLINE: Charlie Patton

SECTION: Pg.

1754:	B-2

LENGTH: 376 words


Baptist Medical Center Jacksonville and Baptist Medical Center South, which
share a license, are the highest-ranked hospitals in 2014 in Jacksonville and
the fourth-highest ranked hospital in Florida in the 25th annual U.S. News &
World Report's Best Hospitals rankings.

1755:	U.S. News ranks the top hospitals in 16 specialties from cancer to urology.

1756:	Here are the rankings for other Jacksonville-area hospitals:

- Mayo Clinic Florida: second in Jacksonville, eighth in the state, 12
high-performing specialties.

1757:	- Wolfson Children's Hospital: one nationally ranked specialty, 48th among
children's hospitals for pediatric neurology and neuroscience.

1758:	Hospitals that had no nationally ranked or high-performing specialities were not
ranked, although information about patient satisfaction rates is included in
U.S. News & World Report's Best Hospitals.

1759:	A total of 144 of the approximately
5,000 U.S. hospitals evaluated were ranked in at least one specialty.

1760:	In a news release, U.S. News said that in evaluating hospitals, "the focus on
the most difficult patients has not changed."

1761:	To see all the rankings, go to http://health.usnews.com.

1762:	SECTION: STATE AND REGIONAL NEWS

LENGTH: 859 words


Jan. 31--Under a tier system being promoted by the state Legislature, patients
on at least two health plans would have to pay more for care at Cape Cod
Hospital in Hyannis than if they went to hospitals in Fall River or Brighton.

1763:	Tiered-plan programs ask insured individuals to pick up a bigger share of the
cost at certain hospitals and doctors' offices in exchange for lower premiums.

1764:	Insurers say they base the ranking system on quality and cost, but local
hospital officials say insurers really consider only costs.

1765:	And those officials argue that the trend toward charging patients higher co-pays
or deductibles at different hospitals is unfair to Cape Cod residents.

1766:	"It's not fair to your membership on the Cape," Jack Lipomi, senior vice
president of managed care at Cape Cod Healthcare Inc., the parent company of
Cape Cod and Falmouth hospitals, said.

1767:	It's not as though patients can walk across the street to another hospital, he
said.

1768:	Cape Cod Hospital has higher costs because the local health care wage index is
high and because it must make up the cost of caring for a large population on
Medicare and Medicaid, he said.

1769:	The reimbursement for those government-funded
programs doesn't cover the cost of care, which is passed on to commercial
carriers, he added.

1770:	"The quality measures are nationally accepted measures," said Tara Murray,
spokeswoman for Blue Cross Blue Shield of Massachusetts.

1771:	There is a level of quality hospitals must meet, she said.

1772:	"Then we tier based
on cost."

1773:	For example, a Blue Options patient with no deductible who gets an MRI at Cape
Cod Hospital, which is in the "basic benefits tier," would pay $250, compared
with $150 at a "standard benefits" hospital such as Caritas Good Samaritan in
Brockton or $75 at an "enhanced benefits" hospital such as Caritas St. Elizabeth
Medical Center in Brighton.

1774:	Blue Options is marketed to employers who want to offer employees plans with
lower premiums, Murray said.

1775:	"We want to provide choices to the employer
community," she said.

1776:	But tiering creates a lot of confusion, said Karen Granoff, senior director of
managed care policy of the Massachusetts Hospital Association.

1777:	Rankings within a
single plan can change from year to year, and different insurers sometimes rank
the same hospitals differently, she said.

1778:	For instance, Cape Cod Hospital is listed in the lowest tiers for Blue Cross
Blue Shield Blue Options and for Harvard Pilgrim's Health Care Independence Plan
marketed to state employees and retirees.

1779:	But both Cape Cod and Falmouth hospitals' inpatient obstetric and adult
medical-surgical programs got top rankings from Tufts Health Plan's Navigator
plan, which means patients would pay the lowest co-pays out of three tiers.

1780:	The plans that also rank doctors heighten the confusion, Granoff said.

1781:	She referred to a case in which a patient paid a $25 co-pay to see a specialist
but had to pay $50 on a follow-up visit to see a lower-ranked partner in the
same practice.

1782:	In health care legislation aimed at individuals and small businesses that went
into effect this month, all health carriers doing business in Massachusetts are
required to offer at least one tiered or "selective" network with a premium rate
at least 12 percent lower than the comprehensive network product.

1783:	"It's one of our fastest-growing products," Murray said.

1784:	But the unpopularity of tiered products on geographically isolated Cape Cod
means that section of the law won't promise much relief to Barnstable County
employers.

1785:	And employers on the Cape -- who are seeing premium increases of 20 percent to
50 percent -- are desperate for relief, said Wendy Northcross, executive
director of the Cape Cod Chamber of Commerce.

1786:	Blue Cross Blue Shield already was one of the higher-priced choices around, she
said.

1787:	"They're all trying to figure out ways to skin the cat."

1788:	To see more of the Cape Cod Times, or to subscribe to the newspaper, go to
http://www.capecodonline.com.

1789:	For more information
about the content services offered by McClatchy-Tribune Information Services
(MCT), visit www.mctinfoservices.com.

1790:	Included as part of the 26th edition of the Best Hospitals list published by the
national magazine this past July, IU Health Goshen was ranked as the fifth-best
hospital in the state out of approximately 170 eligible hospitals.

1791:	U.S. News looked at a number of criteria in the ranking including hospital
reputation, patient survival, patient safety and care-related factors such as
the amount of nurse staffing and the breadth of patient services.

1792:	According to the rankings, IU Health received a "high performing" rating in the
area of hip and knee replacement procedures.

Hospitals ranking utilizes consumer-friendly data and information with the

identify hospitals that excel in treating the most difficult cases.

1794:	"Patients deserve high-quality information on hospitals," Harder said in a

demonstrate excellence in heart attack care by delivering timely, appropriate,

1797:	"Several years ago, we said we wanted to make Goshen the safest place in the

1798:	We are

 In case of any query regarding this article or other content please contact:

helps the organization measure itself against national benchmarks.

1848:	To be considered for the list, hospitals must meet at least one of the following

have at least 200 beds; have 100 or more beds and the availability of four or
more types of technology considered important for a high-quality medical
facility such as a PET/CT scanner and certain precision radiation therapies.

1849:	Next, the hospitals had to meet a volume requirement, individually calculated
for each specialty.

1850:	This narrows the pool of eligible candidates to about 1,800 hospitals.

1851:	According to Avery Comarow, health rankings editor for U.S. News & World Report,

Institute, manages the survey, collects data and crunches the numbers.

1852:	Among other things, Comarow said, "they factor in death rates, reputation,
patient safety and a set of quality-related measures including volume, number of
nurses and the amount of technology that we consider important in that
specialty."

1853:	"We don't include patient satisfaction because there aren't any studies that
show that how happy patients are with a hospital has anything to do with quality
of care," Comarow said.

                                US Official News

Study: Hospital rankings may rely on faulty data

LENGTH: 1281  words

DATELINE: New York



 Washington: American Association for the Advancement of Science has issued the
following news release:



 The October 2016 issue of The Joint Commission Journal on Quality and Patient
Safety, features the article "Consumer Rankings and Health Care: Toward
Validation and Transparency" by Bala Hota, MD, MPH, and co-authors, Rush
University Medical Center (RUMC), Chicago.

1857:	analyzed data on
a sample of hospitals and found that RUMC was not the only organization with
discrepancies in data.

1858:	False-positive event rates were common among
high-transfer and high-volume hospitals.

1859:	article to conclude, "Just as health care providers have ethical and moral
responsibilities to the public they serve, rating organizations and journalists
that grade providers have similar obligations--in their case, to ensure measure
validity and methodological transparency."

1860:	RUMC further explored the importance of rating organization's validity and
methodological transparency in the following essay, "Hospital Rankings Have Room
for Improvement



 Hospital Rankings Have Room for Improvement



 Hospitals that care for sickest of sick found to be unfairly ranked



 When consumers make what can be life-or-death decisions about where to seek
health care, they often look to the numerous online hospital rating services
that are available, including the government-sponsored Hospital Compare,
Consumer Reports, and Leapfrog.

1861:	One reason is that patients vary in terms of how sick they are when they
arrive at the hospital or undergo surgery.

1862:	Older patients with multiple health
problems or advanced diseases are more likely to develop an infection or
complication than younger, healthier patients -- even though they receive the
same quality of care.

1863:	Something didn't add up



 Rush University Medical Center consistently has scored high on patient safety
measures, according to most rankings.

1864:	In fact, Rush University Medical Center
has received nine consecutive "A" grades for safety from the Leapfrog Group,
putting it among fewer than 100 hospitals in the country that have received the
highest possible grade each time since Leapfrog began issuing the semi-annual
ratings in 2012.

1865:	But last year U.S. News "Best Hospitals" issue gave Rush the publication's
lowest ranking for patient safety (one out of five).

1866:	For example, U.S. News data showed that 25 patients at Rush had developed
pressure ulcers, or bed sores, over a particular time period.

1867:	Suspecting a broader problem, they analyzed data on a sample of hospitals and
found that Rush was not the only organization inaccurately unfairly ranked.

1868:	High demand for hospitals' services skew safety scores down



 Two hospital characteristics tended to negatively skew U.S. News patient safety
scores: Taking care of large numbers of patients (i.e., high volumes) or
admitting a high percentage of patients transferred from other hospitals.

1869:	Webb, Hota and Lateef found that the U.S. News data set was missing key
information, such as whether patients with pressure ulcers had these sores when
they were admitted to Rush.

1870:	Large referral hospitals such as Rush, which provide highly specialized care
for the sickest patients and most complex conditions, also tend to have high
volumes and high transfer rates.

1871:	"U.S. News was very responsive to our findings, and the Rush community should
take pride in the fact that we take care of very sick patients very well," says
Lateef, Rush's chief medical officer.

1872:	"When you adjust for patient acuity
(severity of patients' conditions), our outcomes data are among the best in the
country."

1873:	Transparency Is a Two-Way Street



 Lateef and Hota, and the quality team believe that right now the rules used by
hospital rating systems are too complex for most consumers to navigate.

1874:	That's
why they are urging U.S. News and other hospital rating systems to be more
transparent about how they rate hospital quality, so that individual hospitals
can validate the accuracy of their scores and work with the rating organization
to adjust the scores as appropriate.

1875:	In the meantime, Rush will continue to measure, track, and share patient safety
data rigorously, and use that date to drive improvements to make Rush's care
even safer.

1876:	In case of any query regarding this article or other content needs please
contact: editorial@plusmediasolutions.com

LOAD-DATE: October 7, 2016

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

PUBLICATION-TYPE: Newswire


              Copyright 2016 Plus Media Solutions Private Limited
                              All Rights Reserved

                         Telegraph Herald (Dubuque, IA)

                              June 6, 2011 Monday

Study: Hospital rankings might mislead patients

BYLINE: ERIK HOGSTROM TH staff writer ehogstrom@wcinet.com

SECTION: A; Pg.

1917:	2

LENGTH: 819 words


choosing a hospital If not by publication or Internet ratings, how can patients
choose the best hospital?

1918:	"The first step is to make sure the hospital actually
has the services you need -- not all hospitals are created equal," said Russell
Knight, president and CEO of Mercy Medical Center-Dubuque.

1919:	"A second step -- in
noncritical situations -- is to consult with trusted caregivers, such as
physicians, nurses, therapists, pharmacists, etc.

1920:	Of course, most patients will want to choose a
hospital where their preferred physician is comfortable practicing.

1921:	Another step
is to verify insurance coverage.

1922:	Relatively few health plans in our area have restricted networks, but some do,
so it is worth a phone call to the hospital business office, the health plan or
the employer's human resources department to find out if any restrictions
apply."

1923:	Hospital rating services recently took issue with a published medical
report suggesting publicly marketed rankings often fail to identify all
hospitals of equal quality.

1924:	The study in the Archives of Surgery found little
difference in mortality rates for three cancer procedures performed at either
hospitals highly rated by private services or other high-volume hospitals.

1925:	The
study shows for three types of cancer surgery, "America's 50 Best Hospitals" as
ranked by Internet-based HealthGrades, are no better than the other hospitals
once the number of patients they treat is taken into account.

1926:	Based on U.S. News
& World Report rankings, the Top 50 hospitals trumped the rest on just one
surgery: removal of the colon or part of it.

1927:	"Our findings show that both
ratings fail to identify equally well-performing hospitals of similar volume,"
Dr. Nicholas Osborne, of the University of Michigan in Ann Arbor, and colleagues
write in the study.

1928:	"Because these ratings list only a selected number of best
hospitals," the researchers add, "these consumer aids may dissuade patients from
seeking care at a closer, high-volume, equal-quality hospital."

Centers among Bay Area, State and Nation's Best

LENGTH: 599  words


New Delhi, July 21 -- John Muir Medical Center, Walnut Creek has been ranked one
of the nation's top hospitals for the sixth straight year by U.S. News & World
Report in its annual list of America's Best Hospitals.

1952:	John Muir Medical Center,
Walnut Creek was ranked in the Top 50 in Orthopedics, Gastroenterology and
Gynecology, making the medical center one of the top-ranked facilities on the
West Coast in those specialties and the only non-academic medical center in
northern California to appear in the Top 50.

1953:	Fewer than 150 hospitals are
nationally ranked in at least one of 16 medical specialties.

1954:	John Muir Medical Center, Walnut Creek is
listed at #2 out of 45 hospitals in the San Francisco-Oakland area and #7 out of
nearly 400 acute care hospitals in California.

1955:	In total, the two hospitals had 22
services nationally ranked or rated as high performing, including Cancer,
Cardiology & Heart Surgery, Diabetes & Endocrinology, Gastroenterology,
Geriatrics, Gynecology, Nephrology, Neurology & Neurosurgery, Orthopedics,
Pulmonology and Urology.

at community hospitals so residents can stay close to home for the very best
care."

1957:	"These distinctions are a testament to John Muir Health's
team of incredible doctors, nurses, staff and volunteers who every day are
dedicated to advancing our community's health."

1958:	"We have invested significantly in our community through the building of new
patient care towers at both medical centers to be able to care for our patients
for generations to come," said Mike Thomas, president and chief administrative
officer at John Muir Medical Center, Concord.

1959:	"We look forward to continuing to
deliver the excellent care and experience our patients expect and deserve."

1960:	According to U.S. News & World Report, the national and high-performing rankings
showcase only 15 percent of the nation's nearly 5,000 hospitals.

1961:	The hospital
rankings, said U.S. News Health Rankings Editor Avery Comarow, are like a
GPS-type aid to help steer patients to hospitals with strong skills in the
procedures and medical conditions that present the biggest challenges.

1962:	"All of

1963:	"They are where other hospitals send
the toughest cases."

patients.

                       St. Paul Pioneer Press (Minnesota)

                             July 25, 2006 Tuesday

Blue Cross revises ranking system Patients of lower-rated doctors will pay more

BYLINE: JEREMY OLSON, Pioneer Press

SECTION: LOCAL; Pg.

2074:	1B

LENGTH: 500 words


A new health plan from Blue Cross and Blue Shield of Minnesota will make
patients pay more out of pocket if they use hospitals or doctors that provide
lower-quality care at higher costs.

2075:	Blue Cross tried last year to create a "tiered" plan that ranked doctors and
hospitals, but doctors objected because they were judged on their hospital
affiliations and not on their own clinical results.

2076:	The revised plan, announced Monday, measures physician groups on their own
costs.

2077:	Depending on their specialties, they also are evaluated on five to 23
measures of medical quality.

2078:	The resistance among doctors to last year's
proposal was "understandable," said Dr. David Ploncher, Blue Cross' chief
medical officer, but he believes the new two-tier plan provides a fair and open
evaluation.

2079:	In the latest list, some doctors rank differently than their
affiliated hospitals.

2080:	Insurers are hoping tiered plans will lower skyrocketing health costs by
steering patients to top-tier doctors and hospitals, which provide high-quality
care at lower costs.

2081:	Blue Cross is only offering the new plan to employers with self-insured health
benefits, meaning their costs are independent from other Blue Cross users.

2082:	The
rankings do not affect people with Medicare plans or individual health
insurance.

2083:	Two national employers have signed up, Ploncher said.

2084:	Blue Cross projects that employers who select the new tiered plan for 2007 will
see their medical costs decline by 6 percent to 8 percent.

2085:	The savings will
result from patients choosing top-tier doctors or hospitals.

2086:	Hospitals objected last year to Blue Cross' tiering plan, which placed the Mayo
Clinic and the two metro Children's Hospitals in the lower tiers because of high
costs.

2087:	Blue Cross did not change hospital tiers this year, but it plans to
re-evaluate hospitals and clinics on an annual basis.

2088:	Blue Cross is ranking only hospitals and clinic groups in competitive markets.

2089:	An initial list shows that affiliated hospitals and clinics didn't always gain
the same rankings.

2118:	"These (ranked

Suburban hospitals make prestigious list

BYLINE: By Robert McCoppin, Daily Herald Health Reporter
rmccoppin@dailyherald.com

SECTION: NEWS; Pg.

2400:	13

LENGTH: 408 words


Three suburban hospitals -- Edward in Naperville, Central DuPage in Winfield,
and Loyola University Medical Center in Maywood -- were among those joining five
Chicago institutions on this year's U.S. News and World Report list of the
nation's top hospitals.

2401:	In Chicago, three teaching hospitals scored high rankings in several categories.

2402:	The rankings are based on reputation, mortality rate, staffing and services, and
are heavily weighted toward teaching hospitals, in the view of Dr. David Cooke,
vice president for quality and safety at Central DuPage.

2403:	By surveying doctors on where they would refer family members, some institutions
can ride on past reputation.

endoscopies to diagnose disorders like esophageal reflux disease.

2407:	Kelly Leonard, nurse manager for Edward's endoscopy services, said an important
part of the department's success was getting more people screened before they
have a problem.

                               India Pharma News

                       June 27, 2013 Thursday 6:30 AM EST

Axial Exchange: Sharp Memorial Hospital and Doctors Medical Center of Modesto
Earn Top Rankings in Independent California Patient Engagement Index

LENGTH: 508  words


New Delhi, June 27 -- Axial Exchange, Inc., a pioneer in using mobile apps to
deepen the patient's role in improving outcomes, today announced the results of
its California Patient Engagement Index (PEI), a groundbreaking index that ranks
U.S. hospitals based on an analysis of publicly available data in three
categories: personal health management, patient satisfaction and social media
engagement.

2417:	In the California PEI, Sharp Memorial Hospital and Doctors Medical
Center of Modesto topped the list of 135 hospitals in the state.

2418:	This is the
third regional PEI the company has issued; previous PEI's ranked top hospitals
in Florida and Texas.

2419:	Axial Exchange's methodology for ranking hospitals' patient engagement
performance is based on analyzing metrics that research has indicated lower
costs and improve clinical outcomes.

2420:	For each state's PEI, Axial Exchange
evaluates provider organizations' personal health management strategies,
awarding maximum points to those organizations that not only offer electronic
access to patient health records, but also provide resources for ongoing disease
management support, including mobile, tablet and desktop tools.

2421:	Axial Exchange
also ranks hospitals according to an annual Centers for Medicare and Medicaid
(CMS) patient satisfaction survey called the Hospital Consumer Assessment of
Health Plans Survey (HCAHPS), a standardized instrument for measuring patients'
perspectives on hospital care and is also used to determine reimbursement levels
for Medicare patients.

2422:	Lastly, Axial Exchange analyzes the extent to which
hospitals and health systems engage their patients via social networking
channels.

2423:	Lower ranking hospitals in the California PEI
included notable healthcare institutions like Olive View-UCLA Medical Center,
which received a score of 18, and Riverside County Regional Medical Center which
received a score of 13.

2424:	"Through our Patient Engagement Indices, we are starting to see more and more
provider organizations make a commitment to, and investment in, patient
engagement initiatives, demonstrating that healthcare organizations are really
starting to see the value of engagement programs-whether that be via enhanced
social media engagement, mobile health applications or patient education," said
Joanne Rohde, CEO of Axial Exchange.

2425:	"Patient engagement is a real game changer
when it comes to successful health management, and when done right, will
ultimately lead to improve outcomes, better quality care, and reduced costs for
the entire healthcare ecosystem."

2426:	All California
hospitals were notified of their ranking, and provided with the published data
for consideration in their own initiatives toward Meaningful Use and heightened
reimbursement.

2427:	SECTION: STATE AND REGIONAL NEWS

LENGTH: 658 words


April 08--Hazleton General Hospital ranks first in the state in a new measure
that the federal government uses to assess value in health care.

2428:	Through the Affordable Health Care Act, the federal government will increase or
decrease a small percentage of Medicare payments to hospitals based on the
ratings.

2429:	For the past few years, the Hospital and Healthsystem Association of
Pennsylvania used federal data and formulas to let hospitals know how they
compare under value-based purchasing.

2430:	"For Hazleton General Hospital, of 136 hospitals which we have data for, they
ranked No.

2431:	This year, the federal Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services will allocate
$48 million among Pennsylvania hospitals based on the rankings.

2432:	James Edwards, chief executive officer at Hazleton General Hospital, said the
ranking means the hospital will receive an extra $300,000.

2433:	That's about one-quarter of 1 percent of the total revenues for the Greater
Hazleton Health Alliance, which operates the hospital.

2434:	Medicare, the federal health insurance plan for older Americans, paid $36.39
million to Hazleton General in 2011.

2435:	This year's rankings are based on patient satisfaction surveys and what
Ciccocioppo called process measures, such as percentage of patients given
instruction sheets before they are discharged or the share of patients who
receive antibiotics before surgery.

2436:	By 2015, rankings also
will factor in data about hospital-acquired infections and total cost per
Medicare patient from three days before admission until 30 days after admission.

2437:	Previously, Ciccocioppo said, hospitals were not responsible for care given
outside the premises.

2438:	"We have a staff, we have physicians that
are very engaged in doing the best for our patients."

2439:	By another rating, the financial health of Hazleton General improved.

2440:	The hospital alliance earned more than $14 million in each of the two most
recent for which tax forms are available based on gross revenues of $110 million
in 2011 and $107 million the year before.

2441:	For 2012, the alliance said revenues of $114.3 million again exceeded expenses,
but Edwards didn't yet know the amount of profit when interviewed last month.

2442:	He said the improved bond rating allowed the hospital to save money by
refinancing debt.

2443:	The hospital alliance's long-term debt decreased to $21.5 million from $34.7
million between 2010 and 2011, according to the 2011 audit done by the
independent firm ParenteBeard in Wilkes-Barre.

2444:	When refinancing, Edwards said the hospital also allocated money to renovate the
hospital entrance, upgrade equipment and install a system for keeping patients'
records electronically.

UH Rainbow Babies & Children's Hospital in Nation's Top Five for Treatment of
Newborns and Children with Breathing Disorders

LENGTH: 654  words


New Delhi, June 5 -- Rainbow Babies & Children's Hospital at University
Hospitals Case Medical Center once again is ranked as one of the nation's top
children's hospitals, according to U.S.News & World Report's annual rankings of
pediatric hospitals, and for the second year in a row is ranked in all ten
children's specialties.

2446:	For the fifth consecutive year, UH Rainbow Babies & Children's Hospital ranked
among the top five children's hospitals for the care of newborns in its neonatal
intensive care unit.

2447:	"We are proud to once again be recognized as one of the nation's leading
children's hospitals," says Michael Konstan, MD, Chairman of the Department of
Pediatrics at UH Rainbow Babies & Children's Hospital.

2448:	"Rainbow has a rich
legacy in pediatrics and has been ranked as a top children's hospital on this
elite list for 22 years, since the inception of the U.S.News rankings.

2449:	This
prestigious recognition demonstrates our abiding commitment to caring for the
children of Northeast Ohio and beyond."

2450:	UH Rainbow Babies & Children's Hospital's rankings in all ten medical and
surgical children's specialties are:

* #12 in Cancer

* #41 in Cardiology & Heart Surgery

* #11 in Diabetes & Endocrinology

* #50 in Gastroenterology

* #5 in Neonatology

* #16 in Nephrology

* #25 in Neurology & Neurosurgery

* #10 in Orthopaedics

* #5 in Pulmonology

* #26 in Urology

In addition to the top ten rankings, UH Rainbow Babies & Children's Hospital's
pediatric cancer ranking jumped from 22 to 12 this year.

2451:	It comes after the
announcement of the Angie Fowler Adolescent & Young Adult Cancer Institute, made

memory of their daughter, Angie, who passed away from melanoma at age 14.

2452:	"But there is no greater reward than curing children of cancer, and giving them

2453:	The

fragile newborns, from pioneering neonatology in the 1950s to opening the
Quentin & Elisabeth Alexander Neonatal Intensive Care Unit in 2009, the $26.2
million state-of-the-art NICU.

2454:	"Rainbow has a rich history of excellence in
neonatology and we are always looking toward the future, and how we can continue
to enhance the care we provide to our babies."

outcomes and care-related measures, such as nursing care, advanced technology,
infection control and safety measures.

hospital rankings will be published online at www.usnews.com/childrenshospitals.

                 Distributed by McClatchy-Tribune Business News

                              July 6, 2008 Sunday

OPINION: The survey says: We're among the best

BYLINE: Dick Lucinski, Niagara Gazette, Niagara Falls, N.Y.

2538:	Those were the national rankings of the two hospitals in the Niagara Falls area
when it comes to bang for the buck.

2539:	It's a rating of hospitals across America that lists the best value for
the money in health care.

2540:	According to the private firm Data Advantage, LLC, Niagara Falls Memorial
Medical Center is rated first in the state and 12th in the nation when it comes
to providing high-quality, affordable health care.

2541:	The survey rated 1,500
hospitals in the country's 100 largest regions.

2542:	In fact, health
care facilities across the Niagara Falls-Buffalo region littered the Top 100,
bringing our area in as having the best value in health care of any region in
the nation.

2543:	The folks who work at and operate our hospitals are justifiably proud.

2544:	"It's really a tribute to the entire hospital team -- all 1,200 employees and
medical staff," said CEO Joseph Ruffolo of Niagara Falls Memorial Medical
Center.

2545:	The index ranked the hospitals on four main criteria: quality of care,
efficiency of care and affordability, patient experience and reputation as
measured by local public perception.

2546:	For both medical facilities to come out as
high as they did is, as Ruffolo put it, a tribute to all who dedicate themselves
to the care of the ill in our community.

2547:	Ruffolo says the ranking will be a big help in recruiting top-flight physicians
and other medical personnel to the region.

2548:	They say that
it all depends on what you measure; that the criteria used can skew the results.

2549:	Change them a little and it could knock our hospitals down to the middle of the
pack.

2550:	Even if the survey isn't perfect; even if it has some
flaws; 12th or 73rd out of 1,500 is so high on the list that even if the area
hospitals were to be knocked down a bit, it would still leave them among the top
values in health care in the nation.

2551:	To see more of the Niagara Gazette or to subscribe to the newspaper, go to
http://www.niagara-gazette.com/.

                                 Final Edition

Arnprior one of three safest towns for hospital patients: report

BYLINE: Joanne Laucius, The Ottawa Citizen

SECTION: CITY; Pg.

2553:	Arnprior ranked in the top three based on the "hospital mortality index," which
looked at communities in terms of hospital deaths in nine areas, such as
pneumonia and acute stroke, with the figures adjusted for factors such as the
patients' age and sex.

2554:	The municipal rankings, one of hundreds of indicators in the report, are based
the home postal codes of patients.

2555:	They indicate the success of patients' treatment at their local hospitals and at
the larger hospitals that handle more complex cases, said Mark Mullins,
co-author of the report and executive director of the Fraser Institute.

2556:	In all, the report card assessed 50 measures of patient safety and quality of
care for Ontario's 136 acute care hospitals and 138 municipalities using
methodology currently used in a dozen U.S. states and data collected by the
Canadian Institute for Health Information.

2557:	However, only 43 Ontario hospitals agreed to be identified by name on the report
card.

2558:	The first and second top-ranked hospitals in the report and the 10 bottom-ranked
hospitals were among the "anonymous' hospitals.

HEALTHY TALLY U.S. News & World Report gives high ranking to area children's
hospitals

BYLINE: BY REUVEN BLAU NEW YORK DAILY NEWS

SECTION: SPECIAL; Pg.

2631:	2

LENGTH: 754 words


NINE NEW YORK-area hospitals that focus on caring for children battling major
health issues are feeling pretty darn good these days.

2632:	Those hospitals and one in upstate Rochester have just been ranked among the top
50 in the nation in at least one medical specialty, according to U.S. News &
World Report's annual tally.

2633:	Mount Sinai Kravis Children's Hospital was lauded with rankings in six medical
specialty fields, including diabetes and endocrinology, 16th; gastroenterology
and GI surgery, 23rd, and neurology and neurosurgery, 27th.

2634:	The survey is mailed out to 200 children's hospitals
nationwide.

2635:	"We try to make the best use of
that information to provide patients and their families about where to get the
best care for their kids," said Ben Harder, who spearheads the project for U.S.
News & World Report.

2636:	Each year, U.S. News takes a new look at the approaches it takes to rank
hospitals.

2637:	As a result, this is the first year the survey results were based on
a new metric that measures the outcome of heart surgeries at different
hospitals.

2638:	The Society of Thoracic Surgeons developed the new measure.

2639:	"Surgeons and other doctors have told us that's a very important measure,"
Harder said.

2640:	U.S. News also publicly identified the 126 medical advisers who help shape the
survey questions before they are sent to hospital officials.

2641:	"We feel transparency is really important," Harder said, noting publicizing the
names will safeguard any possible questions of preferential treatment to
hospitals tied to those doctors.

2642:	Two New York City hospitals grabbed top spots specifically for their
cancer-treatment units.

2643:	The other city and area hospitals taking top spots are Joseph M. Sanzari
Children's Hospital at Hackensack University Medical Center, N.J.; Bristol-Myers
Squibb Children's Hospital at Robert Wood Johnson University Hospital, N.J.;
Winthrop-University Hospital Children's Medical Center on Long Island; the
Hospital for Special Surgery, Lenox Hill, and University of Rochester-Golisano
Children's Hospital.

2644:	None of the New York-area hospitals landed on U.S. News' elite 10-member honor
roll list.

2645:	Those hospitals are Boston's Children's Hospital; Children's Hospital
of Philadelphia; Cincinnati Children's Hospital Medical Center; Texas Children's
Hospital in Houston; Johns Hopkins Children's Center in Baltimore; Children's
Hospital in Los Angeles; Ann and Robert H. Lurie Children's Hospital of Chicago;
Nationwide Children's Hospital in Ohio; Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh of
UPMC, and Children's National Medical Center in Washington.

2646:	Mount
Sinai Kravis Children's Hospital was praised for its work in six medical
specialties.

2647:	The two New York-Presbyterian children's hospitals get combined
honors in the U.S. News & World Report list.

2648:	2 ranking for 22 years and is still one of only 17
hospitals in the country on the magazine's "honor roll."

objective data.

in their specialty for the toughest cases.

2651:	The hospitals are ranked for 16 medical specialties.

2652:	Other Minnesota hospitals, including Hennepin County Medical
Center and Abbott Northwestern in Minneapolis, also ranked in the top 50 for
various specialties.

2653:	To see the results, visit www.usnews.com/besthospitals.

Abdominal Aortic Aneurysm Repair, Heart Failure, Colon Cancer Surgery, Chronic

2655:	"Mercy is proud to be ranked nationally and recognized as high performing as

Two Baylor Health Care System Hospitals and a Physician Practice Earn National
Award for Excellence

LENGTH: 427  words


New Delhi, Nov. 21 -- Two Baylor Health Care System hospitals and a HealthTexas
Provider Network group are Press Ganey 2013 Beacon of Excellence Award winners.

2673:	Baylor Jack and Jane Hamilton Heart and Vascular Hospital and The Heart Hospital
Baylor Plano are Beacon of Excellence Award winners for inpatient satisfaction.

2674:	The Heart Hospital Baylor Plano also received a Beacon Award for emergency
department satisfaction.

2675:	The Beacon of Excellence Award, formerly the Summit Award, is one of Press
Ganey's most prestigious awards, honoring health care organizations that sustain
an overall patient satisfaction rank above the 95th percentile for at least the
past three consecutive years.

2676:	This is the fourth year in a row that The Heart Hospital Baylor Plano has won
this top award for both inpatient and emergency department satisfaction, which
is 24 consecutive quarters-or six years-of ranking above the 95th percentile in
these areas.

2677:	Baylor Jack and Jane Hamilton Heart and Vascular Hospital has also won the
inpatient Beacon Award for four years in a row, which is 24 consecutive
quarters-or six years-of ranking above the 95th percentile for inpatient
satisfaction.

2678:	"This honor confirms the steadfast commitment these hospitals have to role model
excellence for patients and families on a daily basis," says Terri Nuss, vice
president, Office of Patient Centeredness.

2679:	"Beacon of Excellence Award
recipients set themselves apart from the competition by demonstrating their
dedication to improvement and sustaining excellence in both quality of care and
patient satisfaction.

2680:	Achieving a patient satisfaction ranking above the 95th
percentile for the required three-year period is difficult enough.

2681:	This
award honors health care organizations that have reached the 95th percentile for
patient satisfaction, employee engagement, or physician engagement surveys or
clinical quality performance and is awarded annually based on one year of data.

Maine Medical Center gets national recognition for care;
Its being among the top 50 U.S. hospitals in two specialities shows quality is
close at hand.

2718:	A8

LENGTH: 275 words


Maine Medical Center deserves credit for being listed among the nation's top 50
hospitals for orthopedics and heart care and cardiac surgery.

2719:	But even more noteworthy, that ranking is a sign to Mainers themselves that it
isn't always necessary to go out of state for top-level care.

2720:	The listings were printed in the July 23-30 issue of U.S. News & World Report, a
newsweekly that has carved out a niche for ratings, including its more
controversial rating of colleges and universities.

2721:	In 12 of the 16 categories it uses, hard data such as numbers of cases, death
rates, reputation and care-related factors such as nursing and advanced services
and other statistical categories provide the foundation for its conclusions.

2722:	Out of 5,462 hospitals considered for ranking, only 173 met the standards in one
or more specialities.

2723:	Primarily, it's a tribute to medical professionals at all levels who have worked
long and hard to make MMC the quality institution it is.

                  The State Journal-Register (Springfield, IL)

                           December 12, 2000 Tuesday

Company: St. John's among top 100 facilities in country  ;
 Recognized for stroke, orthopedic health care

BYLINE: TONY CAPPASSO STAFF WRITER

SECTION: LOCAL; Pg.

2725:	9

LENGTH: 401 words


A company that assesses health-care quality ranks St. John's Hospital among the
top 100 hospitals nationwide in treating orthopedic problems and stroke.

2726:	The Springfield hospital made the top 100 based on comparison of cost and other
criteria in the treatment of these patients, according to the Web site,
www.100TopHospitals.com, which is run by the health-care quality assessment firm
HCIA-Sachs.

2727:	St. John's also ended up among the top medical centers that offer orthopedic
surgery residency training, where would-be orthopedic surgeons learn the ropes
of the specialty.

2728:	Only 15 hospitals that offer such training made the top-100 list nationwide and
only one hospital in Illinois other than St. John's, Evanston Northwestern
Hospital, earned the ranking.

2729:	They compared outcomes for patients who had one of four types of orthopedic
surgery: total knee replacement, total hip replacement, partial hip replacement
and open reduction of a hip fracture with a procedure called "internal fixation
of the femoral neck."

2730:	To make it into the survey, a hospital had to have at least 200 patient cases
and at least 30 cases in each of the different surgical procedures.

2731:	"This award is the capstone of a more-than-10-year quality improvement and cost
containment effort," Adair said in a written statement.

2732:	In the stroke rankings, HCIA-Sachs compared outcomes for patients treated for
ischemic strokes, the most common type, using basically the same criteria as in
the orthopedic rankings.

2733:	St. John's scored among the top 100 hospitals that have neurology specialty
training programs.

2734:	Neurologists - doctors who specialize in diseases of the brain and nervous
system - often treat patients who have had strokes, which happen when something
interferes with blood flow to the brain.

2735:	Only 17 hospitals nationwide achieved top-100 status in both orthopedics and
stroke treatment, according to HCIA-Sachs spokeswoman Kerry Lydon-Minton.

2736:	HCIA-Sachs evaluated hospitals based on both clinical and economic data,
Lydon-Minton said.

2737:	How much it cost to provide care was as influential in
placing a hospital in the top 100 as how good the care was, she said.

2738:	The rankings, which came out Thursday, named Johns Hopkins Hospital and
University of Maryland Medical Center has the top two hospitals in Maryland.

2739:	Out
of 70 hospitals in the state, U.S. News and World Report ranked the top 15.

2740:	AAMC earned the highest ratings possible in seven procedures: abdominal aortic
aneurysm repair, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, colon cancer surgery,
heart failure, hip replacement, knee replacement and lung cancer surgery,
according to the rankings.

2741:	U.S. News ranked the top hospitals in every state.

2742:	Both hospitals are a part of the
University of Maryland Medical System.

2743:	BWMC and AAMC are both waiting to hear if the Maryland Health Care Commission
will approve the hospitals' applications for an open heart surgery program.

2744:	The
hospitals submitted for their applications for the certificate of need in
February 2015, following a change to the state health plan for cardiac services.

2745:	Politicians and hospitals officials have been vocal about how long the process
has taken.

2746:	The commission has said it has to carefully analyze the applications
because of the potential for legal challenges from competitors if one or both of
the hospitals are approved to have the program.

Dismal state of govt hospitals

SECTION: EDITORIAL

LENGTH: 344  words


When it comes to world rankings in any sector, Pakistan has hardly ever had
anything positive to write home about.

2764:	It is no wonder then that the country's leaders run to foreign shores as
soon as they need medical treatment; they do not trust the quality of service
offered by hospitals run under their own watch.

2765:	According to this ranking, there
are only four public-sector hospitals among Pakistan's top 20 medical
establishments.

2766:	It is a sorry situation for the majority of our citizens, who do
not have the expensive option of going abroad for medical treatment and must
make do with the local hospital system.

2767:	With
such poor standing and rampant corruption in public-sector health, attracting
grants from foreign donors to improve the dilapidated conditions of our
hospitals is a tall order indeed.

2768:	At the same time provincial and federal
governments seem to think that our tax money is better spent elsewhere.

2769:	The
latest economic survey shows that the health sector received only 0.45 per cent
of GDP during the first nine months of the fiscal year, which is almost
unchanged from FY2014-15, when it stood at 0.42 per cent of GDP.

2770:	According to
the World Bank, Pakistan spends $37 (Rs3,873) per capita on health, which is
lower than the WHO-prescribed level of $44 per capita (Rs4,606) the minimum
spending package required for essential health services.

2771:	It is unjust that our
public office holders head abroad when they need treatment while millions of
impoverished citizens travel hours for subpar treatment at decrepit government
hospitals.

2772:	It is a pity that we have been unable to produce a single world class
health institution in Pakistan's public sector even after almost 70 years of
independence.

2773:	"We're pleased with the way PGRH and northern hospitals in general were ranked
using this particular methodology," said Dr. David Butcher, NH vice-president of
medicine.

2774:	So our first reaction is
we're very pleased with the way PGRH fared."

2777:	Butcher said Northern Health's second reaction is that although the set of
indicators used will provide another way of measuring the quality of care and

2778:	"The hospital mortality index, for instance, is based on a very narrow range of
only nine indicators and because it includes things like death following hip
surgery, it rules out a number of our facilities where that type of surgery is
not done," Butcher said.

OHRH ranked in top seven hospitals in state

BYLINE: Jacob Dick, Messenger-Inquirer, Owensboro, Ky.

2810:	SECTION: STATE AND REGIONAL NEWS

LENGTH: 586 words


Aug. 09--Owensboro Health Regional Hospital has been named among the seven best
hospitals in Kentucky out of 120 in the new rankings of regional hospitals
released from U.S. News and World Report.

2811:	This is the first time the hospital has made the list occupied mostly by large
hospitals in Louisville, Lexington and Covington with health groups stretching
across Kentucky and Ohio.

2812:	U.S. News and World Report ranks all qualified
hospitals in every state, but only awards recognition to hospitals meeting high
standards and for specific treatment areas with high performance.

2813:	"We
weren't focused on this for the ranking, we were focused on this for the
patients and our improvement."

2814:	U.S. News and World Report uses a wide array of factors to organize its rankings
including performance in 16 specialties, patient feedback ratings and health
outcomes across several treatments and conditions.

2815:	The ranking of seven hospitals isn't an arbitrary number used by U.S. News and
World Report.

2816:	Only hospitals meeting a benchmark of standards set by the
publication can be ranked in the state.

2817:	In Kansas, two hospitals out of 150 are
ranked while seven out of 50 are ranked in Connecticut.

2818:	Strahan said being one of the only small regional hospitals to make the list
would serve as reinforcement for some of the engagements the facility has tried
to achieve with its community.

2819:	"It's almost like comparing yourself to a Fortune 500 company, seeing our name
next big hospitals with more resources, academic centers and more centralized
patient populations," Strahan said.

2820:	Owensboro Health was also recognized for high performance in heart failure, knee
replacement and hip replacement procedures.

2821:	The highest scores for these
procedures came from reductions in patient stays, increase in outpatient
procedures, reduction in return stays and quality rehabilitation.

2822:	Strahan said the hospital had specifically focused on those initiatives as a
part of measurements given to hospitals after the Affordable Care Act was
enacted.

2823:	"The ACA has changed the landscape with how we've thought about delivering
health care," Strahan said.

2824:	"We've developed more lean processes, used new
robotic and laparoscopic technology to reduce stays and extended care after
hospital stays."

2825:	According to U.S. News and World report, Owensboro Health Regional Hospital
currently has 356 beds, accepted 68,718 patients through its emergency room,
admitted 16,914 patients and performed 3,888 inpatient and 14,104 outpatient
surgeries last year.

                  The State Journal-Register(Springfield, IL)

                           December 12, 2000 Tuesday

Company: St. John's among top 100 facilities in country ;
 Recognized for stroke, orthopedic health care

BYLINE: TONY CAPPASSO STAFF WRITER

SECTION: LOCAL, Pg.

2867:	9

LENGTH: 476 words


A company that assesses health-care quality ranks St. John's Hospital among the
top 100 hospitals nationwide in treating orthopedic problems and stroke.

2868:	The Springfield hospital made the top 100 based on comparison of cost and other
criteria in the treatment of these patients, according to the Web site,
www.100TopHospitals.com, which is run by the health-care quality assessment firm
HCIA-Sachs.

2869:	St. John's also ended up among the top medical centers that offer orthopedic
surgery residency training, where would-be orthopedic surgeons learn the ropes
of the specialty.

2870:	Only 15 hospitals that offer such training made the top-100 list nationwide and
only one hospital in Illinois other than St. John's, Evanston Northwestern
Hospital, earned the ranking.

2871:	To produce the orthopedic rankings, HCIA-Sachs experts compared care at 1,200
hospitals across the United States by looking at the medical records of more
than 700,000 Medicare patients.

2872:	They compared outcomes for patients who had one of four types of orthopedic
surgery: total knee replacement, total hip replacement, partial hip replacement
and open reduction of a hip fracture with a procedure called "internal fixation
of the femoral neck."

2873:	HCIA-Sachs ranked hospitals based on how much the procedures cost, how long
patients were hospitalized, mortality and complications and the percentage of
patients that doctors discharged home, according to a press release.

2874:	To make it into the survey, a hospital had to have at least 200 patient cases
and at least 30 cases in each of the different surgical procedures.

2875:	"This award is the capstone of a more-than-10-year quality improvement and cost
containment effort," Adair said in a written statement.

2876:	In the stroke rankings, HCIA- Sachs compared outcomes for patients treated for
ischemic strokes, the most common type, using basically the same criteria as in
the orthopedic rankings.

2877:	St. John's scored among the top 100 hospitals that have neurology specialty
training programs.

2878:	Neurologists - doctors who specialize in diseases of the brain and nervous
system - often treat patients who have had strokes, which happen when something
interferes with blood flow to the brain.

2879:	Only 17 hospitals nationwide achieved top-100 status in both orthopedics and
stroke treatment, according to HCIA-Sachs spokeswoman Kerry Lydon-Minton.

2880:	HCIA- Sachs evaluated hospitals based on both clinical and economic data, Lydon-
Minton said.

2881:	How much it cost to provide care was as influential in placing a
hospital in the top 100 as how good the care was, she said.

2882:	Each year, U.S. News & World Report, using an extensive
survey and input from pediatric specialists from around the country, ranks
nearly 200 of the nation's children's hospitals and identifies only the top 50
in each of 10 specialty areas.

2883:	In fact, the Mayo Clinic Children's Center is the only children's
hospital not only in Minnesota but also the surrounding states of Wisconsin,

cancer, cardiology and heart surgery, diabetes and endocrinology,
gastroenterology, neonatology, nephrology, neurology and neurosurgery,
orthopedics, pulmonology and urology.

2884:	"At the Mayo Clinic Children's Center, we take great pride in our ability to

                               The Times (London)

                              May 12, 2003, Monday

Measuring up

SECTION: Features; 17

LENGTH: 641 words


Targets can damage your health.

2992:	The Times reports today that the Government's
system of ranking hospitals is seriously flawed, with some top-ranked hospital
trusts having unnervingly high mortality rates and waiting times.

2993:	While
measuring the performance of such complex organisations is extremely difficult,
it is quite wrong for ministers to judge hospitals primarily against political
targets, rather than clinical outcomes.

2994:	If some second-rate hospitals are being
held up as models for others to follow, this is dangerously misleading as well
as grossly unfair, and will only set back the cause of NHS reform.

2995:	League tables for hospitals, like those for schools, are a sensible element of
the drive to raise standards.

2996:	But their very public nature, and the extra money
and freedom that a high ranking can bring, make it vitally important to measure
what matters as accurately as possible.

2997:	What is being measured when "three star"
status is given to the hospital trust in Walsall that has the highest mortality
rates in the country?

2998:	This cannot be accounted for simply by the fact that certain hospitals
are treating older, riskier patients.

2999:	Barts and University College London, which
have the lowest mortality rates in the country, are teaching hospitals treating
their fair share of high risk patients.

3000:	And the organisation that ranks
hospitals by mortality rates, the Dr Foster unit at Imperial College, London,
standardises as far as possible to take account of such factors.

3001:	Fewer than ten of the 30 criteria used in the
NHS Performance Rankings actually attempt to judge how a hospital affects
patient health.

3002:	In order to be considered for a "three-star" ranking, hospitals have
to perform well on nine targets, all of which are managerial, such as "improving
working lives" and hospital cleanliness.

3003:	It is only after meeting those that
clinical measures such as readmisssion are taken into account, and even these
look only at a small proportion of deaths - from heart bypass or non-elective
surgery.

3004:	The truth is that NHS services are already distorted to meet political targets.

3005:	Many of the hospitals with three
star rankings may have excellent clinical staff, but they are also very good at
managing to meet the targets, which is not the same thing.

3006:	Many of those
hospitals that score well on dealing with outpatients in less than 26 weeks (the
NHS rating) do much less well against the 13-week target used by Dr Foster.

3007:	But
if the Government moved the goal posts, the hospitals would change their
priorities.

3008:	Managers who prefer to be economical with the truth than to upset
Whitehall have good reason: the few who have dared to expose such practices are
regarded by government with deep suspicion and hostility.

3009:	But seemingly scientific indicators can be motivated
more by policy than precision.

                            The Record (New Jersey)

                          September 13, 2005, Tuesday

Hospitals improve 'report card' scores

BYLINE: By Bob Groves

LENGTH: 763 words



Most New Jersey hospitals improved their treatment of heart attack and pneumonia
patients in 2004 compared with the previous year, health officials said Monday.

3046:	Overall, the 82 acute-care hospitals in New Jersey continued to score higher in
caring for heart attack patients than in pneumonia care, though the gap narrowed
last year, according to the state's second annual "report card" on hospital
performance in the two clinical areas.

3047:	"However, hospitals are still missing too many opportunities to give the kind of
care that can improve health and save lives," Jacobs said.

3048:	The New Jersey 2005 Hospital Performance Report scored the hospitals on their
use of eight procedures in treating more than 61,000 patients for whom such
procedures were deemed appropriate.

3049:	The five procedures for heart attacks were
giving aspirin to the patient on arrival and discharge from the hospital, giving
beta blockers on arrival and discharge, and giving an ACE inhibitor upon
discharge.

3050:	Pneumonia scores were based on three care measures: the percentage of eligible
patients whose oxygen levels were checked within 24 hours of arrival, the
percentage who received antibiotics within four hours of arrival and the
percentage who were screened for pneumonia vaccination and immunized, if
appropriate.

3051:	Although about half of the 22 hospitals in Bergen, Passaic, Morris and Hudson
counties slipped in their statewide rankings since last year, the majority
improved their overall scores in this year's report.

3052:	However, a spokeswoman for the New Jersey Department of Health and Senior
Services, which issued the report, cautioned that comparing overall scores is a
better indicator of improvement than hospital rankings, because there are
clusters of several hospitals that share the same scores.

3053:	Not all
hospitals treat patients with all eight care procedures, but were still ranked
with their competitors, he noted.

3054:	Also, patient volumes varied widely between hospitals.

3055:	"It's definitely a helpful tool to see where our challenges are, and where to
improve," said Faillace, a cardiologist and chairman of cardiovascular services
at the hospital.

3060:	The program, set to take effect April 1, measures and ranks Blue Cross'
in-network hospitals on quality, patient experience and cost.

3061:	"We will re-evaluate hospitals

3062:	The letter states that any of Blue Cross' customers in the tier-ranking program

                       Vallejo Times Herald (California)

                            March 14, 2007 Wednesday

A healthy, welcome dose of self-policing

BYLINE: By Times-Herald editorial staff

SECTION: OUR VIEW

LENGTH: 490 words


Nothing is more nerve wracking or fearsome as the loss of one's health, and
going to the hospital for treatment should not make those fears more frazzled.

3080:	Yet, based on their own records many California hospitals, including those
serving Vallejo residents, have yet to understand fully how their operations
before the scalpel, as well as during and after surgery, greatly heighten or
soothe patient anxiety.

3081:	That is why we applaud the recent launching of a statewide Web site that
compares hospitals in some of the most critical forms of treatment patients
face.

3082:	While the new Web site tends to oversimplify treatment rankings and is based on
relatively old data, it nevertheless serves as an important notice to hospitals
that they can no longer merely rely on perception to win patient confidence.

3083:	The hospitals already appear to be getting the message.

3084:	Kaiser officials say they provide excellent care for heart
patients, but until recently were admittedly inconsistent in how they recorded
that care on patient records.

3085:	Given their new procedures, their scores will rise
much higher, they insist.

3086:	A doctor who helped develop the ranking system said, however, that all
participating hospitals not only knew the methodology but accepted it.

3087:	We have no reason to doubt that the two hospitals as well as the 200-plus others
on the Web site will continue to work for improvements.

3088:	Any improvements will obviously bolster the hospitals' operations.

3089:	More
importantly, any reforms will work to provide not only better, more consistent
healthcare but also will ease concerns of most patients who have little or no
choice of hospitals they enter or doctors who treat them.

3090:	We commend the self-policing aims of the rankings, the participation of the
hospitals and hope there is a genuine growth of which areas are studied.

3091:	The
California Hospital Assessment and Reporting Taskforce should also be commended
for working with focus groups to determine an easy and understandable way for
those outside the medical profession to access this information.

3092:	Consumers will
benefit mostly, but improvements in the most critical of treatments should
trickle down to the most basic, and that's a very healthy development.

                            March 30, 2011 Wednesday

Madison County ranks low for health

BYLINE: April Abernathy, The Herald Bulletin, Anderson, Ind.

3218:	-- Madison County isn't the healthiest place to live in
Indiana, but it's getting better -- relatively speaking.

3219:	County Health Rankings released reports early Wednesday morning ranking Madison
County 75th, improving from last year's ranking of 79th, out of 92 Indiana
counties in overall health.

3220:	"We're up four from last year," Stephenie Grimes, public health coordinator of
Madison County, said.

3221:	The health rankings are part of the Mobilizing Action Toward Community Health
projects, according to the County Health Rankings website.

3222:	The rankings compare the health in more than 3,000 counties and the District of
Columbia.

3223:	Other health factors such as smoking and obesity, clinical care and social,
economic and physical environments were researched.

3224:	People are nearly twice as likely to be in fair or poor health in the
unhealthiest counties, according to a press release from County Health Rankings.

3225:	Unhealthy counties have significantly lower high school graduation rates, twice
as many children in poverty, fewer grocery stores and farmers markets and higher
rates of unemployment.

3226:	With a high percentage of smokers and adult obesity and a high number of
sexually transmitted diseases, Madison County ranked 91st in health behaviors,
leading county health officials, hospitals and clinics to create initiatives to
improve health.

3227:	Grimes said through the Public Health System Quality Improvement Process
(PHSQIP), the State Health Department sponsored the Madison Health Partners
Board, a group of health officials from local hospitals, to come together for
four weeks to share where the county was ranked, educate, inform and work
together.

3228:	"They had to choose a health initiative and a system plan," Karesa Knight,
executive director, Intersect Inc., said.

3229:	"The initiative was smoke-free air and
the system plan was to build on  more cohesive health in the community."

3230:	"We have worked with businesses including the City of Anderson and Red Gold to
train people to hold classes for their employees," Knight said.

3231:	"In 2009 (and)
2010 we received quality of life grants to work with physicians, dentists and
anyone in the health field to refer to the quit line," Knight said.

3232:	She helped work with health service providers to implement the "two A's and R":
Ask at every visit if the patient uses tobacco, assess them if willing to quit
and refer them to either a pharmacy for medication.

3233:	"If a health provider encourages a patient to quit, they are twice as likely
to," she said.

3234:	Area hospitals like St. John's Hospital are recruiting primary and specialty
care physicians in addition to expanding with dialysis centers, oncology,
endocrinology and geriatrics.

3235:	Beyond physician and specialty care recruitment, screening services and
assistance to those underinsured or not insured have increased.

3236:	"At Community, we offer weight management classes and programs for adults
and kids, routine free health screenings for our community, support groups and
educational classes, smoking cessation programs and countless other
opportunities for individuals to better their health."

3237:	VanNess added that it is the job of the hospital to offer these types of
opportunities and more to patients.

3238:	The problem is that the hospital cannot
force a person to alter his or her choices and choose a healthier lifestyle.

                                   L2 Edition

How your hospital fared on state report card

BYLINE: By Robert McCoppin Health & Fitness Reporter rmccoppin@dailyherald.com

SECTION: NEWS; Pg.

3242:	1

LENGTH: 733 words


Patients can compare rankings of Illinois hospitals for a wide range of
performance and cost measurements on a new Web site that debuted Thursday.

3243:	The Illinois Hospital Report Card, for the first time, ranks hospitals and
surgery centers based on quality of care, infections, complications, staffing
and cost, along with other criteria.

3244:	The site, by the Illinois Department of Public Health at
healthcarereportcard.illinois.gov or at idph.state.il.us, was so popular it
crashed twice before getting back up.

3245:	Consumers can choose which hospitals and which criteria they want to compare by
facility, county, or geographic radius.

3246:	Advocate Good Samaritan Hospital in Downers Grove, for instance, ranked in the
top three among local hospitals in all three quality of care measures, which
rated how often a hospital followed recommended treatments for pneumonia, heart
attack and heart failure patients.

3247:	Many hospitals struggled with patient satisfaction, with the rankings among
local hospitals ranging from 76 to 44 percent who were highly satisfied.

3248:	Central
DuPage

Hospital in Winfield topped the list among local hospitals, while Adventist
GlenOaks in Glendale Heights came in lowest.

3249:	The national average for highly satisfied patients is 64 percent.

3250:	Jacqueline Conrad, chief nursing officer at Adventist GlenOaks in Glendale
Heights, noted that the survey results are a year old, and said her hospital has
made great strides trying to improve patient satisfaction since then.

3251:	GlenOaks started a program last year to build morale and engagement among
front-line caregivers, training employees to introduce themselves and describe
their background to patients, and explain any procedures they're about to go
through.

3252:	"We recognize the patients are at their most vulnerable when they come to a
hospital, and we want to reduce their anxiety," Conrad said.

3253:	Some criteria seem quirky but mean a lot to patients.

3254:	Among the local hospitals,
Delnor Community in Geneva kept patient rooms the quietest at night and Centegra
Northern Illinois Medical Center in McHenry responded to call buttons most
consistently.

3255:	Advocate Good Shepherd Hospital in Barrington came up as the lowest cost among
local hospitals for numerous procedures.

3256:	Advocate Condell in Libertyville often
came out on the top of the cost list.

3257:	So many factors go into cost, Good Shepherd spokesman Mike Deering said, it's
hard to explain why.

3258:	But he said hospitals are very mindful of cost, avoiding
unnecessary stays and unnecessary tests, for instance.

3259:	The cost data on the site is probably the least useful, because most fees are
negotiated lower with insurers or patients, said Ian Jones, vice president for
clinical performance at Sherman Hospital in Elgin, which came in around the
middle of most cost comparisons for local hospitals.

3260:	In general, he said of the rankings, "Anything that sheds light on medical care
and outcomes that patients can access is a good thing."

3261:	After years of operating in obscurity, hospitals are increasingly being judged
online at a variety of sites.

3262:	Also Thursday, at the national level, America's Health Rankings rated Illinois
29th among states for its overall health, crediting us for a high number of
primary care physicians, but dinging us for pollution and a high rate of binge
drinking.

3263:	Those rankings are based on community behavior, the environment, health policy
and clinical care.

3264:	Consumers also can find performance data at the Hospital Compare Site at the
Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services, and the Illinois Hospital
Association is working on a Web site to compile patient safety, satisfaction and
performance data.

Berkshire Medical Center earns top ratings for care

BYLINE: Berkshire Eagle Staff

SECTION: BUSINESS; Headlines; Health

LENGTH: 477 words


PITTSFIELD -- Berkshire Medical Center has received five-star ratings and top
national rankings for clinical quality from HealthGrades, the leading
independent health care ratings organization.

3266:	The recognition is based on HealthGrades' 12th annual Hospital Quality in
America study, which analyzes patient outcomes at virtually all of the nation's
hospitals, using publicly available data.

3267:	In the 2010 HealthGrades ratings, BMC achieved national recognition for cardiac
services, pulmonary services, stroke services and critical care services.

3268:	BMC
was honored with the 2010 Pulmonary Care Excellence Award, achieving a ranking
among the top 5 percent in the nation for pulmonary services, and the 2010
Stroke Care Excellence Award, ranking BMC among the top 10 percent in the nation
for stroke services.

3269:	"These accomplishments relate to important services for our community in areas
of care that our families and friends will likely need at some point in their
lives," said Diane Kelly, chief operating officer of BMC.

3270:	"When we are
recognized for being the absolute best in an area like critical care, our
patients can be confident that the same level of commitment to high quality care
will be there for them in all of our services."

3271:	"It is clear from the continuing national recognition, as found in the latest
HealthGrades rankings, that patients of Berkshire Medical Center receive the
highest quality care, on a par with the best hospitals in the country," said
David Phelps, president and CEO of Berkshire Health Systems.

3272:	The HealthGrades study, the largest annual report of its kind, analyzed patient
outcomes in nearly 40 million Medicare hospitalization records from 5,000
hospitals over the years 2006, 2007 and 2008.

3273:	This year's study found:

n Across all 17 procedures and diagnoses in which mortality was studied, there
was an approximate 72 percent lower chance of dying in a five-star rated
hospital compared to a one-star rated hospital, and a 52 percent lower chance of
dying in a five-star hospital compared with the national average.

3274:	n If all hospitals performed at the level of a five-star rated hospital across
the 17 procedures and diagnoses studied, 224,537 Medicare lives could
potentially have been saved from 2006 through 2008.

3275:	HealthGrades' hospital ratings and awards reflect the track record of patient
outcomes at hospitals in the form of mortality and complication rates.

3276:	HealthGrades rates hospitals independently based on data that hospitals submit
to the federal government.

3277:	More information on the HealthGrades study, can be found at www.healthgrades.com
.

Primary named one of top children's hospitals in nation

BYLINE: By Lisa Rosetta The Salt Lake Tribune

SECTION: LOCAL

LENGTH: 410 words


Primary Children's Medical Center was ranked among the top 30 children's
hospitals in the country based on a three-part mix of reputation, low death rate
and other care-related factors, according to U.S. News & World Report.

3279:	Children's hospitals, which in the past were lumped together with all other
hospitals in the news magazine's annual ranking, were examined in a stand-alone
report published in its Sept. 3 issue, which hit newsstands Monday.

3280:	The top three hospitals were Children's Hospital of Philadelphia, Children's
Hospital Boston and Johns Hopkins Hospital in Baltimore.

3281:	Primary Children's - which was ranked 18th overall - received the highest score
of all children's hospitals in the category of death rate, meaning its mortality
rates for key procedures were lower than the 113 hospitals who responded to the
news magazine's survey.

3282:	Each hospital's mortality was ranked on a 0 to 6 point scale, based on the
mortality rates and the total number of key procedures.

3283:	Primary Children's was the only children's hospital
receiving the full 6 points.

3284:	Among the procedures considered were heart and neurosurgery operations, as well
as bone marrow transplants.

3285:	Hospitals had to meet a minimum volume requirement
for each procedure and achieve an acceptable death rate to receive points.

3286:	"This recognition in U.S. News and World Report confirms something that we've
known for years - that we have one of the best children's hospitals in the
country," said Primary Children's CEO Joe Mott in a news release.

3287:	The hospitals' scores for reputation were based on a survey of 100
board-certified pediatricians, adolescent medicine specialists and
neonatologists.

3288:	A medical facility was considered eligible for this year's rankings if
classified by the National Association of Children's Hospitals and Related
Institutions as a free-standing children's hospital or as a children's hospital
within a hospital - a large multidisciplinary pediatric service within a medical
center.

3289:	In March, 122 hospitals met this standard, and 113 responded to the
survey.

3395:	Unlike with some health-care award programs, hospitals do not pay any money to

3396:	The company analyzes hospitals
nationwide using data that hospitals generate when caring for Medicare patients.

3397:	St. John's last placed among the top 100 hospitals for heart care in 1999.

3401:	The company also applies mathematical formulas to adjust for variations between

3402:	Solucient then compares each hospital's performance to the performance of their

3403:	The company doesn't reveal which of the top 100 hospitals

3404:	Instead, it breaks down the top hospitals into three

                             March 6, 2007 Tuesday

Web site grades hospitals on quality of care, satisfaction

BYLINE: Kevin Smith, San Gabriel Valley Tribune, Calif.

3486:	The Web site -- the result of a partnership between the California HealthCare
Foundation, UC San Francisco Institute for Health Policy Studies, and California
Hospitals Assessment and Reporting Taskforce -- offers comprehensive information
on more than 200 hospitals, representing 70 percent of all hospital admissions
in the state.

3487:	Hospitals are rated on 50 performance indicators, including patient satisfaction
and specific conditions ranging from maternity services to heart disease
treatment.

3488:	"We worked with consumer focus groups to make sure we designed the Web display
in a way that would work for patients and families," said Maribeth Shannon, who
directs the Market and Policy Monitor program for the California HealthCare
Foundation.

3489:	"The benefit of this Web site is that it encourages improvement in
hospitals."

3490:	Huntington Hospital in Pasadena earned an average quality-of-care rating for
treatment of heart attack and superior rankings in the areas of patient
satisfaction and timeliness of care.

3491:	Seventy-eight percent of heart attack patients indicated they would recommend
the hospital.

3492:	In the maternity patient satisfaction category, 91 percent of patients said they
would recommend Huntington Memorial.

3493:	Kaiser Permanente Baldwin Park Medical Center garnered an above-average rating
in the quality-of-care category for heart attack and a superior medical patient
satisfaction ranking.

3494:	R. Adams Dudley, associate professor of medicine and health policy at UC San
Francisco and project director for the Institute for Health Policy Studies, said
the Web site ratings are adjusted for differences in the patient mix.

3495:	"When you do patient surveys, for example, maternity patients are always happier
than other types of patients," he said.

3496:	Surgical patients likewise tend to be happier than medical patients because
"when they leave the hospital they go home with their problem fixed," Dudley
said.

3497:	Presbyterian Intercommunity Hospital in Whittier nabbed a superior rating for
heart attack care and a superior patient satisfaction ranking.

among 173 in the country to earn a spot in U.S. News & World Report's "America's
Best Hospitals" guide, on newsstands today.

3517:	"It is always gratifying to be recognized as one of the best, and this really
reflects the hard work and dedication of our faculty and staff," said David
Entwistle, University Hospitals and Clinics' CEO.

3518:	The magazine initially evaluated 5,462 hospitals based on three standards:
membership in the Council of Teaching Hospitals, affiliation with a medical
school and the availability of at least nine out of 18 key technologies, such as
shaped beam radiation and advanced cancer therapy.

3519:	To be ranked among the top 50 in a specialty, such as gynecology, a hospital had
to meet one of two requirements: It must have treated a minimum number of
Medicare or Medicaid patients who had specific conditions or underwent certain
procedures during a three-year period, or it had to be named among the best by
at least one physician in the latest three annual surveys.

3520:	Of the 173 hospitals that are ranked in this year's guide, 18 earned "honor
roll" status by ranking at or near the top in at least six specialties.

3521:	Pediatric hospitals will be ranked separately in the Sept. 3 issue of U.S. News
& World Report, on newsstands Aug. 27.

Health Insight has ranked Wesley Medical Center number one among hospitals in
Mississippi in Quality Measures for 2008.

3539:	Health Insight calculates national rankings for hospitals using publicly
reported data from the Centers for Medicare & Medicaid Services (CMS) Hospital
Compare web site.

3540:	The Hospital Compare data contains hospital-specific
performance on 25 quality measures for over 4,200 hospitals nationwide.

3541:	The
quality measures show how often hospitals give recommended treatments that are
known to get the best results for patients.

3542:	Information about these treatments is taken from the patients'

3543:	Hospitals voluntarily report their data, and some hospitals may not
provide data for some topics or measures.

3544:	Not only was Wesley Medical Center ranked first in Mississippi, the hospital
