                           October 10, 2009 Saturday

BMC gets high mark

BYLINE: By Tony Dobrowolski, Berkshire Eagle Staff

SECTION: NEWS; Health; Headlines

LENGTH: 556 words


Sunday, Oct. 11PITTSFIELD -- Berkshire Medical Center has been ranked first in
both the state and New England in a nationwide study conducted by a Tennessee
firm that measures health-care value.

3599:	Nationally, BMC was ranked 86th out of the 4,500 hospitals that were considered
for the 2009-2010 Hospital Value Index by Data Advantage LLC of Nashville, a
privately held health-care information company that has specialized in providing
the healthcare and business communities with independent and objective
information about the country's hospital industry since 1992.

3600:	Only acute-care general service hospitals that participate in the Medicare
reimbursement program were considered for the Hospital Value Index, said Data
Advantage's senior adviser John Morrow.

3601:	Specialty hospitals, veterans hospitals,
and private health organizations that don't participate in federal programs were
excluded, he said.

3602:	The rankings are based on each hospital's quality, affordability, efficiency and
patient satisfaction performance under the Hospital Consumer Assessment of
Healthcare Providers and Systems (HCAHPS) regulations.

3603:	The index is designed to use only objective,
verifiable, and quantitative data that are consistent and complete across the
country to ensure objective measurement rather than anecdotal evidence,
according to Data Advantage.

3604:	"I'm happy to see that we have
valuation from external organizations such as the index not just in top
performance in quality, but having done so in a cost-effective manner, which is
critical for any health-care system."

3605:	"These national achievements for Berkshire Medical Center are a testament to the
dedication of our medical staff, employees, trustees, and leadership team, and
reinforce our commitment to providing the highest level of quality care for our
patients," Phelps said in a written statement.

3606:	The company's previous rankings were focused on hospitals that are located
in the country's 100 largest metropolitan statistical areas.

3607:	Before the advent of the Hospital Index, no public measurement existed to
integrate efficiency, affordability and quality, Ellrodt said.

3608:	"In my mind what's exciting about this award and the Data Advantage approach is
to be able to look at quality and at what cost," Ellrodt said.

                               India Pharma News

                      September 9, 2013 Monday 6:30 AM EST

MedCenterDisplay: Patients Prefer Digital Communications to Print

LENGTH: 455  words


New Delhi, Sept. 9 -- Three out of four patients and caregivers who viewed
hospital messaging on digital screens in eight hospitals in the U.S. found the
content enhanced the hospital experience and provided health information they
could use, according to a study by media and marketing research firm Arbitron,
Inc.



MedCenterDisplay, a leading provider of digital patient engagement networks for
hundreds of hospitals in the U.S., commissioned Arbitron to conduct on-site
interviews of adults who had viewed hospital and health-related information
running on flat screen monitors placed in hospital waiting areas and cafeterias.

3619:	"We believe these findings clearly reinforce the value in establishing a robust
patient engagement network that brings patients and hospitals together to
improve healthcare," said Gregg Tarquinio, Ph.D., chief executive officer of
MedCenterDisplay.

3620:	"Already hospitals that communicate key healthcare delivery
messages on digital screens are seeing lower readmissions and higher 'HCAHPS'
patient satisfaction scores."

3621:	Those surveyed found information on hospital news, general health news, and tips
on maintaining good health most interesting, ranking the information from 4.0 to
4.6 on a scale from 1 to 5.

3622:	These rankings mirror recent reports that healthcare
has become one of the highest search topics online.

3623:	The report also found that only one-third of all visitors to the hospital were
actually patients.

3624:	Two-thirds were family members and caregivers of patients,
which correlates with national estimates on the rising number of adult
caregivers reaching nearly 45 million, according to AARP.

3625:	"In any patient engagement effort, hospitals have a real opportunity to reach
out to everyone involved in a patient's long-term recovery process," said
Tarquinio.

                              March 5, 2007 Monday

Web site grades hospitals on quality of care, satisfaction

BYLINE: By Kevin Smith Staff Writer

SECTION: BUSINESS

LENGTH: 440 words


Finding a good plumber or mechanic can be relatively easy.

3676:	The Web site - the result of a partnership between the California HealthCare
Foundation, UC San Francisco Institute for Health Policy Studies, and California
Hospitals Assessment and Reporting Taskforce - offers comprehensive information
on more than 200 hospitals, representing 70 percent of all hospital admissions
in the state.

3677:	Hospitals are rated on 50 performance indicators, including patient satisfaction
and specific conditions ranging from maternity services to heart disease
treatment.

3678:	"We worked with consumer focus groups to make sure we designed the Web display
in a way that would work for patients and families," said Maribeth Shannon, who
directs the Market and Policy Monitor program for the California HealthCare
Foundation.

3679:	"The benefit of this Web site is that it encourages improvement in
hospitals."

3680:	Huntington Hospital in Pasadena earned an average quality-of-care rating for
treatment of heart attack and superior rankings in the areas of patient
satisfaction and timeliness of care.

3681:	Seventy-eight percent of heart attack patients indicated they would recommend
the hospital.

3682:	In the maternity patient satisfaction category, 91 percent of patients said they
would recommend Huntington Memorial.

3683:	Kaiser Permanente Baldwin Park Medical Center garnered an above-average rating
in the quality-of-care category for heart attack and a superior medical patient
satisfaction ranking.

3684:	R. Adams Dudley, associate professor of medicine and health policy at UC San
Francisco and project director for the Institute for Health Policy Studies, said

said.

                     The Telegraph-Journal (New Brunswick)

                             April 19, 2013 Friday

Good hospitals, or bad statistics?

3729:	A9

LENGTH: 700 words


The CBC's Fifth Estate recently broadcast Rate My Hospital, a "sweeping
cross-country investigation into Canada's hospitals.

3730:	Interviews of health system administrators trying to explain their poor ratings
only added to the sleaziness.

3731:	Yorkton Regional Health Centre spokesperson Sharon Tropin did an admirable job
explaining a D rating, criticizing the rating tool for having only five
indicators.

3732:	The Fifth Estate's website indicates the statistical methodology was developed
by biostatisticians noted for their work in evaluating health system
performance.

3733:	Here's how this data torture took place:

Hospital report card grades were determined by measuring the number of standard
deviations a hospital is from the average for all hospitals.

3734:	(Standard deviation
is a measure of the variability in the data.)

3735:	The expected operating range of any process is +/- 3 standard deviations.

3736:	Combined with the system of grades, this data torture transforms measured
ratings into ordinal rankings.

3737:	The CBC's report card proves nothing more than that, in a
ranking of hospitals (or anything else), some will be at the top, some at the
bottom, and about half will be below average.

3738:	The report card
standard deviations are the probabilities of obtaining a performance rating,
assuming hospitals are the cause.

3739:	The CBC measured the probability that a person would die
after jumping off Toronto's CN Tower.

3740:	They are ubiquitous
in reporting from provincial health systems, quality councils, health ministries
and the Canadian Institute for Healthcare Information.

3741:	But shouldn't the media
be exposing scientifically incompetent practices rather than promoting them?

3742:	The health-care system in Canada has enough problems.

3743:	Robert Gerst is a columnist for Troy Media and partner in charge of operational
excellence and research &statistical methods at Converge Consulting Group Inc.

3744:	He is author of "The Performance Improvement Toolkit: The Guide to
Knowledge-Based Improvement" and numerous articles in peer-reviewed
publications.

3745:	Dr. Loren Roth, chief medical officer of the University of Pittsburgh Medical
Center, distributed a letter to local physicians last month in which he
encouraged them to vote early and often for UPMC hospitals should they be among
the lucky doctors asked to participate in U.S. News & World Report magazine's
annual survey of "best hospitals."

3746:	Noting the medical center's recent success in the ranking by U.S. News -- UPMC
was rated this year as one of the 14 best hospitals in the nation -- Dr. Roth
told his physicians that: "We would like to build on this upward momentum.

3747:	"In the survey you may list one hospital, several hospitals, or all of
them."

3748:	While Dr. Roth was not available for comment yesterday, spokesman Frank
Raczkiewicz said health system officials have sent similar letters for the past
three years or so to remind physicians about the survey, and encourage their
participation.

3749:	Mr. Raczkiewicz did not say how many physicians received the letter, which
encourages doctors who are surveyed by U.S. News to alert the marketing office
at UPMC so that officials can "update our database."

3750:	The September letter
encouraged doctors to submit their surveys in time for the Halloween deadline.

3751:	The U.S. News ranking has become a serious business for hospitals, which often
incorporate accolades from the magazine in their own marketing campaigns.

3752:	As
such, it's not surprising that hospitals including UPMC would work to get out
the vote, said Betsy Gelb, a professor of marketing and entrepreneurship at the
Bauer College of Business at the University of Houston.

3753:	Some experts in health care quality have long bemoaned the public attention
given to the magazine's rankings, in part because U.S. News uses a hospital's
reputation among physicians as one of its three main data points.

3754:	A Dartmouth
College study published in Health Affairs during 2004, for example, raised
questions about the magazine's methodology by finding that Medicare patients
with similar chronic conditions receive strikingly different care among
hospitals identified as "best" in geriatrics by U.S. News.

3755:	"They need
to really shift the ratings away from reputation and toward hard evidence --
that the right outcomes are being achieved, and the right processes are being
followed."

3756:	Dr. Loren Roth, chief medical officer of the University of Pittsburgh Medical

Advantage Health Plan, for 120,000 state employees and dependents.

3844:	And starting
it all, HealthPartners introduced a tiering system a decade ago that intertwined
best medical practices with costs.

3845:	All health-care consumers will benefit if insurers and providers use this new
system to measure not only cost, but value.

3846:	Focusing solely on cost might make
fiscal sense in the near term, but would almost certainly cost employers and
individual consumers more in the long run.

3847:	CHANGE IS NECESSARY

Changes in the health-care industry are imperative.

3848:	Patients, doctors, insurance
companies -- all must take more active roles if Minnesota is to retain its
reputation for high-quality health care.

3849:	The "tier" ranking system, when it
focuses on quality and cost in a format easily accessible and understandable to
patients, is one promising approach.

3850:	The subject arose earlier this month when Blue Cross and Blue Shield came under
fire for one aspect of its proposal to put providers in one of two tiers, with
patients paying more for access to "second tier" doctors and hospitals.

3851:	Doctors
objected to being categorized based on whether or not they were affiliated with
the lower-cost Blue Cross-preferred hospitals.

3852:	Earlier this month, Blue Cross
decided to drop doctors from its rankings, focusing instead on hospitals and
clinics.

3853:	We're glad Blue Cross, as the state's largest health insurer, stepped
back to reconsider its methodology of doctor ranking.

3854:	The financial efficiency
of a hospital is important, but doctors' rankings should be more than a
reflection of a hospital ledger.

3855:	Ultimately doctors, too, will need to make changes if a statewide tier system is
to work.

3856:	HealthPartners, for example, created a model for diabetes treatment
based on best practices recommended by the Institute for Clinic System
Improvement.

3857:	The hospitals and clinics that had the best outcomes -- fewer diabetes-related
amputations or heart disease for example -- received more compensation.

3858:	Those
providers were also graded on cost containment.

3859:	An effective tier system underscores best practices and rewards practitioners
for their use of them.

3860:	CONSUMERS HAVE ROLE, TOO

Keep in mind patients, also, must become better advocates for themselves.

3861:	Most
patients are unaware of the best way to treat a disease, yet medical research
shows certain treatments are highly effective.

3862:	Patients will need to become more
proactive and ask about best medical practices with the same ease today that
they ask about drugs advertised on television.

3863:	While we're pleased to see more and more insurers move to a tier system that
evaluates costs and outcomes, we'd like to see more consistency and agreement on
effective treatment.

3864:	Everyone -- consumers, providers and insurers -- have a role in reshaping the
health care system.

3865:	Health plans must make their information to patients more
transparent.

3866:	Insurers have to establish bonds of trust with medical providers so
accurate information can be freely exchanged.

3867:	And consumers need to ask more
questions and make more demands for the best medical practices.

3868:	The new rankings, released today, recognized the Rochester-based health care
facility for its "breadth of excellence."

3869:	1 in eight of the 12
specialties, while also claiming the top spot for such categories as reputation,
mortality index, patient safety, nurse staffing and Magnet status, patient
services and technology.

3870:	"This basically demonstrates why Mayo has the most trusted name in health care
globally," Noseworthy said.

3871:	"That's why patients come from 140 countries and 50
states."

3872:	Mayo sits atop the rankings thanks to its stellar performance in the individual
specialties that were measured by the magazine.

3873:	U.S. News & World Report has been compiling its list of best hospitals for 27
years, but didn't actually declare an overall No.

3874:	Mayo was ranked as the top health care facility in three different states:
Minnesota, Arizona and Florida.

3875:	While the main Minnesota campus is in the midst
of an ambitious $6 billion, 20-year expansion project dubbed Destination Medical
Center, Noseworthy specifically cited Mayo's Florida campus as a site that's
made major strides in recent years.

3876:	He says Mayo's
top rankings are critical to attracting new patients from across the globe, many
of whom consult the U.S. News & World Report rankings before deciding where to
seek treatment.

3877:	However, the news won't change anything from the initial vision and mission of
the Mayo brothers.

3878:	"We've been the most trusted name in health care for a very long time, according
to market research," Noseworthy said.

LENGTH: 436 words


June 10--AKRON -- Akron Children's Hospital, with two facilities in Boardman, is
ranked nationally in seven pediatric specialties, according to U.S. News & World
Report's 2014-15 Best Children's Hospitals report.

3903:	Of the 89 ranked hospitals, 10 -- including three in Ohio -- earned a place on
the Honor Roll, according to the magazine report.

3904:	A 372-bed children's general facility with 9,453 admissions in the most recent
year reported, Akron Children's performed 3,316 annual inpatient and 12,025
outpatient surgeries.

3905:	The information used to determine the rankings is provided by the American
Hospital Association, which compiles data on all U.S. hospitals through an
annual survey and from other sources.

3906:	U.S. News says in a release that while relatively few children face
life-threatening or rare conditions or have to go through complicated
operations, some do, and they need expertise that a typical hospital, where
nearly all inpatients are adults, cannot provide.

3907:	Nor do most hospitals
see large numbers of children with wide-ranging cancers or respiratory illnesses
or kidney conditions.

3908:	And even among hospitals that do, the reality, as it is
for hospitals that treat adult patients, is that some places are better than
others.

3909:	That is why U.S. News, in 2007, began using data to rank medical centers on
their ability to help the children who most need it, according to the release.

                The Berkshire Eagle (Pittsfield, Massachusetts)

                            December 17, 2010 Friday

BMC earns honors

BYLINE: By Amanda Korman, Berkshire Eagle Staff

SECTION: NEWS

LENGTH: 551 words


Saturday December 18, 2010

PITTSFIELD -- Berkshire Medical Center has been ranked as one of the top
hospitals in the state and in the upper echelon nationally in several categories
in a study conducted by a national organization.

3941:	The independent study by Health- Grades, a health care ratings group, lists the
Pittsfield hospital in the top 10 percent in the nation in both stroke care and
pulmonary care.

3942:	In Massachusetts, BMC was ranked first among hospitals in the treatment of
stroke and critical care services.

3943:	HealthGrades rates 5,000 hospitals nationwide, and institutions cannot choose
not to be rated.

3944:	In the past two years, BMC has hired four new neurologists who have improved
care for the 225 to 250 patients each year who have a stroke or a stroke
warning, according to Gray Ellrodt, the hospital's chief of medicine.

3945:	Critical care services encompass multiple conditions, the most common of which
is sepsis, or overwhelming infection in the bloodstream.

3946:	The hospital has slipped in the rankings in gastrointestinal medical treatment.

3947:	This
year BMC was given a poor, or 1-star, rating gastrointestinal surgeries and
procedures category.

3948:	Kelly said that what drove those numbers down was not the surgeries themselves,
but that patients were developing pressure ulcers afterward.

3949:	BMC has since taken
measures to reduce those complications, she said.

3950:	HealthGrades issues star ratings for 26 procedures and medical treatments:
5-star ratings indicate a mortality or complication rate below the national
average; 3-star means performance is as expected; and 1-star illustrates a
mortality or complication rate higher than the national average.

                      The Atlanta Journal and Constitution

                       May 21, 1995, Sunday, ALL EDITIONS

SPECIAL ANNUAL REPORT;
THE GEORGIA 100;
THE BEST OF BUSINESS;
Software company thrives on change in health systems;
Feeding growth: HBO & Co. wants to link the separate facilities of giant medical
networks with its computer programs.

3975:	It has several elements: hospitals and clinics, health insurance, physician
practices, home health care, pharmacies, long-term care - all connected by
arrows.

3976:	The Atlanta-based company wants to link the separate facilities of giant medical
systems with its financial and clinical software.

3977:	Anything from a patient's
medical history to insurance claims information could be called up, at any site,
with a click of a button.

3978:	Right now, those networks provide just a small piece of HBOC's revenue.

3979:	But as
hospitals and other medical providers are forced to get more efficient, McCall
sees it as a big growth area.

3980:	That would continue to feed a steep growth curve HBOC has built since 1991.

3981:	Last year, HBOC's revenue increased 30 percent and earnings 50 percent.

3982:	2 among providers of information systems and services in health care, bearing
down on No.

3983:	HBOC said it would buy
Charlotte-based Health Systems Group, a unit of First Data, for more than $ 180
million in stock.

3984:	Stock 'reasonably valued'

The company's stock price has followed the growth curve, though it has been
accompanied by a high price-to-earnings ratio.

3985:	And it holds a large share of the market of
hospitals with 500 or more beds - those expected to survive and lead the rush to
consolidation.

3986:	For clients, HBOC's core products can track patient care, along with radiology,
pharmacy and laboratory operations.

3987:	HBOC's financial systems cover billing and
collections, accounting and budgeting.

3988:	Such data help hospitals make decisions on cost and patient care quickly and
accurately.

3989:	HBOC also provides staff to run hospitals' data systems, and sells computer
hardware.

3990:	But last year's revenue spurt came not just from these core products but from
new products and acquisitions.

3991:	Earlier this year, HBOC acquired a company specializing in laboratory
information systems, and struck a deal to buy Pegasus Medical, which has
developed a hand-held, computer-based patient records system, primarily for
doctors' offices.

3992:	In 1991, the year he joined
HBOC from CompuServe, HBOC lost $ 3.4 million on revenue of $ 170.7 million.

3993:	"I think McCall has a very good feel of both computer markets and health care
markets," said Alan Jenks of Jenks Healthcare Business Report, an industry
newsletter.

3994:	Revolution

Another plus for the company is the revolution revamping the payment scheme in
health care.

3995:	Under the old rules, hospitals felt little pressure to be cost- effective.

3996:	The
more medical services performed, the more revenue generated.

3997:	Now, hospitals and other medical providers are being forced to be more efficient
than ever - to improve quality of care and drive costs down, he said.

3998:	First, technology to help hospitals handle managed
care premiums and payments has great potential, he says.

3999:	In addition, many hospitals have consolidated to provide metrowide care systems.

4000:	The push is not just to have access to financial data but to connect medical
providers with clinical information.

4001:	"Once you link up with all providers, you need to communicate electronic medical
records," said analyst Steven Halper of Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette.

4002:	Thus, HBOC's health-network diagram is important to the company's future, Halper
added.

4003:	"There's a certain amount of risk," including possible problems with new
services, or being late to market with a product.

4004:	This story features HBO & Co., a health
care software and information systems company, ranked number 3 among Georgia's
Top 100 businesses.

                                 FIRST Edition

Medicare penalizes 3 Valley hospitals

BYLINE: Michelle Merlin

SECTION: NEWS; A; Pg.

4244:	3

LENGTH: 732  words


Three hospitals in the Lehigh Valley are taking a financial hit for being among
facilities nationwide with infection and complication rates that the federal
government considers high.

4245:	The ranking means that Medicare is reducing payments to those
hospitals by 1 percent for one year - a period that began in October.

4246:	This year 769 of 3,203 hospitals reviewed were penalized, up from 751 last year,
according to the federal Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services.

4247:	St. Luke's University Health Network's Miners campus in Schuylkill County,
Geisinger Medical Center in Montour County and the University of Pennsylvania
and Thomas Jefferson University hospitals in Philadelphia also are among the 40
facilities in Pennsylvania facing cuts.

4248:	The report, released in December, is part of the Affordable Care Act's Hospital
-Acquired Condition Reduction Program, which seeks to get hospitals to stem
preventable conditions such as surgical-site infections, post-operative hip
fractures, sepsis and accidental lacerations.

4249:	The
biggest reductions were for bad reactions to medicines, catheter infections and
post-surgical blood clots.

4250:	For Lehigh Valley Health Network's Cedar Crest campus, the penalty will result
in a loss of more than $1 million, said Dr. Anthony Ardire, LVHN senior vice
president for quality and patient safety.

4251:	This is the third year the hospital is
in the bottom quartile, according to data from Kaiser Health News that looked
at which hospitals were on the list in past years.

4252:	Ardire lauded efforts to make hospitals safer.

4253:	Smaller community hospitals,
he said, tend to have healthier patients who get released faster, reducing the
chance for hospital-acquired conditions.

4254:	"It penalizes hospitals with the sickest patients that get all the referrals,
with the regional burn centers and the regional trauma centers," Ardire said.

                      The Woodinville Weekly (Washington)

                                November 4, 2013

EvergreenHealth recognized among best hospitals in the nation by Healthgrades

BYLINE: The Woodinville Weekly staff

LENGTH: 446  words


EvergreenHealth recently announced it has earned several distinctions for 2014
across many of the health care system's specialties from Healthgrades, the
nation's leading independent provider of information to help consumers make
informed decisions about a physician or hospital.

4310:	This places EvergreenHealth among the top 5 percent of hospitals in the nation
for clinical excellence in each of those specialties.

4311:	To determine the rankings, Healthgrades evaluated nearly 4,500 hospitals
nationwide for 31 of the most common inpatient procedures and conditions and
identified the 100 best hospitals within each procedure.

4312:	"Delivering the highest level of quality care and service to our patients and
their families is at the very heart of everything we do," said EvergreenHealth
CEO Robert Malte.

4313:	"Recognitions like this underscore that commitment to our
community, and it's the hard work of each physician, provider, nurse, staff
person and volunteer that helps us achieve excellence in these and many other
specialties and practices across the organization."

4314:	In addition, EvergreenHealth is also among the top 10 percent of hospitals in
the nation for the treatment of stroke for the fifth consecutive year.

4315:	A 5-star rating indicates the hospital
consistently helps patients achieve better-than-expected outcomes.

4316:	Other 2014 5-star rankings, which place EvergreenHealth in the top 15 percent of
hospitals in the nation, include recognition for treatment of heart failure,
pneumonia and sepsis; and for gastrointestinal care such as treatment of GI
bleed and pancreatitis and for both colorectal surgery and appendectomy
procedures.

4317:	According to Healthgrades, if all hospitals had performed similarly from 2010 to
2012 to hospitals receiving 5 stars, on average 234,252 lives could potentially
have been saved and 157,418 complications could potentially have been avoided.

4318:	For more information visit www.evergreenhealth.com/quality.

Tuen Mun Hospital worst in surgery;
Improvements demanded as ratio of unexpected deaths after operations worsens
after the facility demonstrated headway in the previous year

BYLINE: Elizabeth Cheung

SECTION: /EDITORIAL/CONTENT/SCMP/NEWS/CITY; nws city front.pgl; Health; Pg.1

LENGTH: 464 words


Tuen Mun Hospital ranked worst among all public hospitals for the sixth time in
surgical performances, with the highest ratio of unexpected deaths after
surgery.

4333:	In the Hospital Authority's latest report on surgical performances, the hospital
in Tuen Mun had the highest ratio of fatalities for scheduled operations at
2.202 - meaning the actual number of deaths was a little more than double the
number of patients expected to die after surgery.

4334:	Princess Margaret Hospital in Kwai Chung performed the worst in emergency
operations, with a fatality ratio of 1.48.

4335:	Of the 13 hospitals doing emergency operations, Ruttonjee Hospital was
considered the best with a fatality ratio of 0.298.

4336:	For scheduled operations,
there were 17 hospitals ranked in the survey, of which Tuen Mun was the worst.

4337:	"The report was not a ranking, but just [a way] to evaluate the performance of
individual hospitals and look for improvement for less satisfactory hospitals,"
said Professor Paul Lai Bo-san, director of the authority's surgical outcomes
monitoring and improvement programme.

4338:	The report, which recorded around 25,000 surgeries between July 1, 2014 and June
30 last year, measured surgical performance by a ratio of actual fatalities over
expected number of deaths.

4339:	Lai said no reasons were found by data analysis to explain the two hospitals'
poor performances.

4340:	However, he believed taking in extra patients for vascular surgeries could
explain the problem at Princess Margaret Hospital.

4341:	Lai said that in the past two years some patients who were originally scheduled
at other hospitals within the Kowloon West cluster were transferred to Princess
Margaret Hospital.

4342:	"Our targeted measures were once effective," said Lai.

4343:	To meet growing demand in New Territories West, Pok Oi Hospital in Yuen Long
would add 15 sessions of operations each week this year.

4344:	Tsang Kin-ping, vice-chairman of Hong Kong Patients' Voices, said the authority
should take more aggressive action to improve performance at Tuen Mun.

4345:	"If the low ranking were to repeat, not only
services but staff morale would be affected too."

                         Portland Press Herald (Maine)

                       May 15, 2005 Sunday, FINAL Edition

Hospitals taking notice of BIW's rankings;
The company aims to improve quality of care and reduce costs for its 16,000
workers and dependents.

4355:	F1

LENGTH: 1451 words



During the next few weeks, about 16,000 Bath Iron Works employees and their
dependents will get a report card from the company ranking local hospitals on
subjects ranging from in-patient costs to infection control.

4356:	BIW officials said this is the second year they have put out such a ranking, and
they hope it will help their workers and dependents become savvy health-care
consumers.

4357:	They also hope it pushes hospitals to improve to better serve their
workers.

4358:	In a bottom-line way, having employees healthy and working rather than living
through an extended hospital stay due to medical errors makes good business
sense.

4359:	What I've
been most encouraged by is the hospitals' behavior."

4360:	Leveraging a 16,000-strong population of health consumers ensures that southern
Maine hospitals have to pay attention.

4361:	"It clearly has the potential of changing referral patterns almost overnight,"
said Dr. George L. Higgins III, chief medical officer and vice president for
medical affairs at Maine Medical Center in Portland.

4362:	Hospital officials said they support the idea of educating consumers about their
health-care choices, but the officials also noted that a static report card has
limitations.

4363:	In some cases, the data - which BIW took from independent state and national
groups that specialize in examining hospitals - tell only part of the story.

4364:	"It is very early in the evolution of how to present this and what it means,"
said Dr. Stephen Sears, chief medical officer at MaineGeneral Medical Center,
which has hospitals in Augusta and Waterville.

4365:	The concept of transparency in hospitals is a hot topic in the industry, said
Higgins.

4366:	Consumers want to be better informed about their hospitals before they
go in for non-emergency care.

4367:	That information can be particularly helpful in an
area like southern Maine, where consumers have a number of hospitals to choose
from that are geographically convenient.

4368:	As in almost every business, health care is one of the single most significant
costs for BIW.

4369:	"I was convinced early on that the kind of saber rattling around premium costs
really wasn't the issue," Gildart said.

4370:	"You have to get past the insurance
piece of it and start paying attention to what's really going on in the
health-care arena and look at the behavior of all the parties."

4371:	"If you can improve quality,
other things start to fall in place, like cost, etc."

4372:	BIW officials began meeting with hospital executives several years ago to
discuss expectations - what a major employer wants from a health-care provider.

4373:	On the report card, the company also points to other reasons why it undertook
the project:

A 1999 study from the Institute of Medicine estimated that each year up to
98,000 people nationwide die from preventable medical errors in hospitals.

4374:	In 2004, Maine hospitals reported 15 deaths due to medical errors.

4375:	It's logical that BIW should have expectations of hospitals, said Higgins.

4376:	The
company expects its employees to take part in wellness programs, quit smoking
and do other things to improve their health, he noted, and expects hospitals to
provide great care by physicians with cutting-edge skills.

4377:	BIW's report card ranked eight hospitals.

4378:	Gildart suggested that the overall rankings
of the hospital didn't matter much; some hospitals received lower ratings in
certain areas because they choose to specialize in niche services and not in
areas that the rating groups place more importance on.

4379:	Information on the card from the Maine Hospital Association ranks patient
satisfaction surveys and evaluates treatment patients got for heart attacks and
heart failures, said Sears.

4380:	That information, which is outcome-based, is a valid
measurement, he said.

4381:	Less valid, in his opinion, are rankings from the Maine Health Management
Coalition because it evaluated processes (not outcomes), and information was
collected through hospital surveys and was not validated.

4382:	The MHA looks at seven elements, and one of them has to do with having
an effective smoking cessation counseling program for patients.

4383:	But the
reason the hospital does less is because it has some very expert
gastroenterologists who have perfected procedures that eliminate the need for
the surgeries, he said.

4384:	Higgins said Maine Med will have to further develop its on-line connectivity
with its potential patients.

4385:	As consumers consider the hospital for a procedure,
the Web site can have full information about the staff, the operation,
statistics, and other data.

4386:	Gildart said the card wasn't meant to be an "infallible measurement."

4387:	Also, he
said, it is likely to change over time as more information becomes available,
and as hospitals and ranking organizations have further dialogue regarding
what's fair and important information to study.

4388:	Lesko said measuring the monetary benefit of an initiative like this is
impossible, because predicting the cost of health care from one year to the next
is equally challenging.

4389:	But a program like this could show benefits 10 years down the road as overall
health-care quality in Maine rises, helping not only BIW's work force, but the
entire state.

4390:	It is intended to help the company's employees
and dependents make informed decisions about where to obtain the best health
care in southern Maine.

4391:	"These report cards are
making us do the hard work in making sure that we're delivering on what they
want," said Dr. George L. Higgins III, chief medical officer and vice president
for medical affairs at Maine Med.

4392:	Copyright 2005 Blethen Maine Newspapers, Inc.

                              June 1, 2012 Friday
                                  City Edition

Schools wrestle lack of rural doctors

BYLINE: SILAS ALLEN, Staff Writer

SECTION: NEWS METRO/STATE; Pg.

4431:	12A

LENGTH: 722 words


Officials at Oklahoma's two medical schools are moving forward with a plan they
hope will combat a shortage of doctors in rural Oklahoma.

4432:	The Oklahoma Hospital Residency Training Program Act provides just over $3
million to create residency programs at hospitals in rural, underserved areas
across the state.

4433:	Under the program, the Oklahoma Health Care Authority would contract with the
Oklahoma State University College of Osteopathic Medicine in Tulsa and the
University of Oklahoma College of Medicine in Oklahoma City to establish and run
new residency programs in medically underserved areas.

4434:	Although those details are still being worked out, Howard Barnett, president of
OSU-Tulsa and the OSU Center for Health Sciences said the university is already
in discussions with a few rural hospitals that are interested in participating
in the program.

4435:	Barnett said he wouldn't name the hospitals that have expressed interest until
those discussions are finalized, but he said most of them are located in towns
with populations of about 25,000.

4436:	Under those programs, students could be granted
conditional admission to medical school as college sophomores, provided they
maintain certain standards.

4437:	Recruiting students who come from rural areas originally is important, she said,
since they tend to be more inclined to return to rural Oklahoma to practice once
they graduate.

4438:	The rankings cite a high prevalence of smoking and obesity, limited availability
of primary care doctors and low use of prenatal care for the low ranking.

4439:	A New England Journal of Medicine article ranks Oklahoma as the state that faces
the most challenges in meeting medical needs.

4440:	According to the article, Oklahoma is expected to see a large expansion in the
Medicaid population as the federal health care law takes effect.

4441:	But Oklahoma
doesn't have the primary care capacity to deal with those newly insured
patients.

4442:	Without outside efforts, the demand for medical care could outstrip
the supply of providers in the state.

4443:	Davis, a former rural hospital administrator, said hospitals in rural areas face
certain challenges that their urban counterparts don't see.

4444:	Doctors in rural
hospitals face tougher call schedules, simply because their staffs are smaller.

4445:	Doctors' spouses may also face limited employment opportunities in rural areas,
she said.

4446:	All those factors make it more difficult for hospitals in rural areas to attract
doctors, she said.

4447:	As a result, rural Oklahoma sees its physician population
shrinking, even as graduates from its medical schools leave the state for their
residencies.

4448:	By exposing students to rural Oklahoma during medical school and bringing them
to rural hospitals for residencies, Davis said, the program will help make them
more aware of the opportunities there.

4449:	That could mean the difference between
sending doctors to rural Oklahoma or to other states.

4450:	"We've already spent a lot of money educating these people.

                          Sunday Times (South Africa)

                                November 7, 2004

Bill may curb private healthcare

BYLINE: Adele Shevel

SECTION: Economy, Business & Finance; Pg.

4460:	27

LENGTH: 374 words




Hospitals could be coming to the end of their strong run

ADELE SHEVEL

THE big three private hospital groups, accused by the government of making
"super profits", are again highly ranked in the Top 100 Companies.

4461:	But hospitals may be coming to the end of their strong run, given the regulatory
pressure that could reduce their huge earnings.

4462:	The National Health Bill, which has been signed by President Thabo Mbeki, is
aimed at regulating the services of private healthcare.

4463:	This includes the issuing of "certificates of need", which will require doctors
and healthcare facilities such as private hospitals to apply for permission to
practise in a specific area.

4464:	Hospitals are particularly vulnerable because of
the high levels of investment required to set up and run their operations.

4465:	Pressure is also coming from medical schemes and their administrators, which are
looking at ways to counter the power of private hospital groups.

4466:	The Council for Medical Schemes, the body that regulates medical schemes, says
the private hospital market is highly concentrated.

4467:	The council's view is that medical schemes need to offer a consistent set of
benefits to members.

4468:	The hospitals have the power to put forward increases which medical schemes
don't have the power to counter.

4469:	If schemes refuse to accommodate the demands of
hospitals, the hospitals have the power to make members pay out-of-pocket.

4470:	This
leaves members irate with the medical schemes.

4471:	*Government intervention in private healthcare is gaining momentum.

4472:	Legislation
was passed recently to make drugs more affordable and promote the use of generic
equivalents.

4473:	The medical-schemes industry is more regulated to prevent it from
discriminating against "high-risk " individuals - the aged and the sick.

                      The Vancouver Sun (British Columbia)

                               May 6, 2011 Friday
                                 Final Edition

Ontario eyes name/shame hospital ranking; Similar scheme has been used in
Britain, but not without difficulties

BYLINE: Pauline Tam, Postmedia News, With A File From Andrea Woo, Vancouver Sun

SECTION: CANADA & WORLD; Pg.

4492:	B1

LENGTH: 701  words


The Ontario government is considering a provincewide ranking system for
hospitals that would provide information that would allow patients to compare
hospitals, reward hospitals for good performance and identify poor performers.

4493:	The "name-and-shame" scheme, designed to give patients a clearer picture of
service quality, would be modelled after a short-lived one in Britain, which was
shown to have improved hospital standards, according to documents obtained by
the Ottawa Citizen.

4494:	No such scheme is under consideration in B.C., according to Ministry of Health
spokeswoman Michelle Stewart.

4495:	Under the proposed Ontario system, hospitals would be given a rating of up to
three stars for their performance in areas such as treating patients safely and
effectively, reducing surgical and emergencyroom waits and improving
cleanliness.

4496:	Another system under consideration would simply rate hospitals "excellent,"
"good," "fair" or "weak," according to documents released under
freedomof-information laws.

4497:	However, it's not clear if the
government has adopted the idea.

4498:	In recent years, Ontario hospitals have gradually adopted ways of measuring
their own quality and making their leaders responsible for improving patient
care.

4499:	Last month, new rules kicked in that linked a portion of executive pay to
how well hospitals took care of patients.

4500:	The new rules require the province's 154 hospitals to publicly post annual
reports detailing their progress in meeting a long list of "quality indicators,"
ranging from getting more staff to wash their hands when dealing with patients
to reducing rates of inhospital infections.

4501:	The Ontario government sees the new accountability rules as a way not only to
improve care and rein in executive salaries, but also to provide taxpayers with
a standardized way to compare hospitals and the care they deliver.

4502:	A ranking system would appear to be the next logical step.

4503:	It could also lay the groundwork for more sweeping changes to how hospitals are
funded, with the goal of rewarding those that are more efficient and treat
patients safely and effectively.

4504:	After Britain introduced a star-rating system in 2001, hospitals showed a
noticeable improvement in how well they cared for patients.

4505:	High-performing hospitals were rewarded with more funding and financial freedom
from the government, allowing them the right to borrow money and organize
services to meet local needs.

4506:	At facilities with few or no stars, corporate decisions were scrutinized more
closely, and executives were told they could lose their jobs if they didn't do
better.

4507:	A major downside was that the star-rating system did not take into account the
key measure of whether a hospital's finances were being managed well.

4508:	As a result, the system encouraged low-performing hospitals to spend their way
out of trouble, resulting in massive deficits.

4509:	The reports released to the Ottawa Citizen also indicated that some British
hospitals manipulated their data to look like they had improved standards when
they actually hadn't.

4510:	For example, to meet the target of a maximum four-hour wait for treatment in
emergency departments, some hospitals made patients wait in ambulances outside
the hospital until staff were confident they could be treated.

4511:	The reports concluded that services excluded from the ranking system tended to
be neglected by hospitals.

4512:	As a result, "a low rating in itself will not necessarily encourage
organizations to improve on areas where improvement may be needed if there is no
measurement in place for those areas."

4513:	In 2005, amid political and union opposition to the starrating system, the
British government scrapped the scheme in favour of a two-tiered system that
measured quality of patient care and use of resources.

4514:	Instead of stars, hospitals were given a rating on a fourpoint scale ranging
from excellent to weak.

4515:	LOAD-DATE: May 6, 2011

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

GRAPHIC: Ted Rhodes, Postmedia Files; While Ontario is considering using a
hospital ranking system, no such scheme is being looked at in B.C., the Ministry
of Health says.

4516:	PSS, a medical distribution company, employs about 3,200 company-wide, more than
450 locally.

4517:	The Nasdaq Global Select Market has the highest initial listing standards of any
exchange in the world based on financial and liquidity requirements.

4518:	In the 2006 rankings, 50 medical centers are named as America's best in the
category of Heart and Heart Surgery.

4519:	St. Vincent's is ranked 47 and is one of only two Florida hospitals named among
the top 50.

4520:	This is the third time St. Vincent's has been ranked among the
nation's finest heart hospitals.

4521:	Out of the 5,189 hospitals evaluated, only 176 scored high enough to be ranked
in any specialty.

4522:	Urvaksh Karkaria/The Times-Union

EBAY LOSES KEY EXECUTIVE; FACES STIFF COMPETITION

EBay Inc. faced a double blow Thursday as it announced a key executive's plans
to leave and an analyst said Google Inc.'s new online payment service represents
a bigger threat than expected to the Internet auction company's health.

4523:	He said in
an interview that he wants to spend more time with his wife and two children.

4524:	He will be replaced by Rajiv Dutta, who has been with the company since 1998,
serving as chief financial officer, head of strategy and president of Skype, the
Internet phone service eBay acquired last year.

4525:	The San Jose-based company's shares fell $1.51, to $26.85 on the Nasdaq Stock

4615:	In the 100 Top Hospitals list released today by Truven Health Analytics, UMHS is

4617:	"We are very proud of this honor because it recognizes our extensive efforts to
continually improve the quality and safety of care we provide to patients," says

4618:	"It's gratifying to see how the hard work of our physicians and staff has

Cuomo takes on Empire, HIP/GHI on doc ranking

Attorney General Andrew Cuomo is targeting two of Long Island's biggest health
insurance companies in his ongoing scrutiny of doctor-ranking programs.

4691:	An Oct. 18 letter sent by the AG to Empire said physician-ranking programs run
by insurers could be a conflict of interest, "because of the insurers' financial
motive to steer consumers to the cheapest, and not necessarily the best,
doctors. "

4692:	The letter also says Empire's program could be "driven by the desire
to save money for the insurer" and "not necessarily designed with the best
interest of consumers in mind. "

4693:	Greiner said Empire would not release quality
and cost data "until we are comfortable that we have appropriately addressed
questions raised by stakeholders such as New York consumers, employers,
physicians and regulators, including the New York attorney general. "

4694:	Going electronic at Nassau University

It may not be the richest hospital on the planet, but Nassau University Medical
Center is hyping its efforts to stay on the "cutting edge of health-care
technology," in the words of Arthur Gianelli, president and chief executive of
Nassau Health Care Corp.

NUMC has implemented a new picture-archiving and communication system that
allows staff to digitize and transmit medical images such as ultrasounds and
X-rays.

4695:	The hospital also recently went live with a document-scanning system
that enables staff to view medical records electronically, part of an industry
trend toward digitizing medical records.

4696:	A total of 2 percent of the hospital's annual budget is spent on IT -
approximately $5 million, according to hospital spokeswoman Shelley Lotenberg.

4697:	In addition, NUMC received $1.8 million this year to update IT systems through
the state's Health Care Efficiency and Affordability Law, Lotenberg said.

Academic urges public rankings

BYLINE: CAROL NADER, HEALTH REPORTER

SECTION: NEWS; Pg.

4788:	8

LENGTH: 510 words


HOSPITALS should be given star ratings based on factors such as death and
infection rates, to improve performance and help patients make better informed
choices, a leading health academic says.

4789:	Giving the best hospitals a five-star rating would be one option.

4790:	Another would

4791:	Professor Swerissen, a senior adviser to Carmen Lawrence when she was federal
health minister, said areas that could be rated included surgery mortality
rates, medical errors, readmission rates and negligence when a court had found
the hospital was at fault.

4792:	He said public reporting would improve the quality of the health system and
could reduce complications and mishaps.

4793:	"I am very keen to see the health system have much stronger reporting on patient

in to how they were ranked.

effectively it's being spent, and I also think it helps patients make more
informed decisions and choices between hospitals," he said.

4799:	Deakin University dean of
health sciences John Catford said league tables could have a demoralising effect
on hospitals and might not be helpful.

4800:	He said hospitals might be prompted to choose patients who were more likely to
get well, to improve their rating.

4801:	The Australian Medical Association Victorian branch president, Mark Yates, also
believed the proposal could give hospitals a "perverse incentive" to
discriminate against patients at greater risk of dying.

out of 100, placing it eighth out of the 36 Minnesota hospitals ranked by the

4848:	"Regardless of what the rankings are, I think safety is always a high priority

4849:	Since readmissions in
hospitals can often mean that there was a problem during the initial stay,
RARE's goal is to encourage hospital practices that will result in 4,000 fewer
avoidable hospital readmissions from July 2011 to December 2012 -- a 20 percent
drop in the state's readmission numbers.

4850:	As such, LRHC encourages
patients to ask questions about their post-hospital treatment, and it sends out

4851:	As the hospital's chief "infection preventionist,"
Bowman's job has many facets: she educates staff on the procedures they need to
follow to avoid in-hospital infections, she makes sure those procedures are
being followed, she tracks information on infections in the hospital, and she
investigates the circumstances surrounding any infection that occurs.

4852:	"You either use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer or wash your hands in a sink
when you come to work, when you come into a patient room, before you touch a
patient, after (taking) a pair of gloves off, and after touching a patient," she
said, noting that those steps alone go a long way in reducing infections.

4853:	Another way the hospital reduces infections is through a series of practices
called "bundles."

4854:	When conducting a surgical procedure, all involved staff
follow a series of steps to make sure the surgery site remains sterile.

4855:	One of the most sensitive procedures involves the placement of a central line IV
-- an IV that is routed to the chest.

4856:	Since central line infections can
essentially result in blood poisoning, the bundle for central line procedures is
in-depth, involving a draped area, sterilized gloves and gowns, masks, head
coverings and a daily check on the IV entry point.

4857:	"Our patients are older and sicker, and the older you are and the sicker you are
and the more medical problems you have, the higher your risk of infection," said
Bowman.

4858:	Her response and LRHC's response is to work even harder to find ways to take the
risk out of hospital stays.

R.I. hospitals tops for heart treatment

BYLINE: By Felice J. Freyer

LENGTH: 1075 words



Rhode Island hospitals are leading the pack nationwide in measurements of how
well they treat heart attacks, heart failure and pneumonia.

4860:	According to new federal data on more than 4,200 hospitals around the country,
Rhode Island's 10 acute-care hospitals -- as a group -- rank number one overall
in how often they take certain steps known to produce the best outcomes for the
three conditions studied.

4861:	That doesn't necessarily mean that Rhode Island has the best hospitals.

4862:	The
measures focused on narrowly defined aspects of care, not everything that a
hospital does.

4863:	But the state's high ranking does reflect the success of the
hospitals' longstanding efforts, instigated by a 1998 state law, to work on
those measures of quality.

4864:	"We certainly are a shining star when you look at the comparison across the
country," said Marcia K. Petrillo, chief executive officer of Quality Partners
of Rhode Island, an agency hired by the federal government to monitor and
improve the care of Medicare beneficiaries. "

4865:	Overall the Rhode Island
hospitals are providing some of the best care in the country, for these
conditions."

4866:	"I think we have some of the better hospitals in the country," agreed Dr. David
R. Gifford, the acting state health director who was previously medical director
at Quality Partners.

4867:	The U.S. Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services last week unveiled Hospital
Compare (www.hospitalcompare.hhs.gov), a new Web tool that compares how often
some 4,200 hospitals around the country followed widely accepted guidelines for
the care of heart attacks, heart failure and pneumonia.

4868:	The state has been publishing the same information about Rhode Island hospitals
since 2002, but this is the first time that meaningful national comparisons have
been available.

4869:	The federal government did not study how patients fared -- whether they died or
got better -- because the severity of a patient's condition can affect the
outcome as much as what the hospital does.

4870:	Instead, the government looked at
"the process of care," identifying 17 actions that have been proven to increase
a patient's likelihood of surviving those three conditions.

4871:	Those actions include giving heart-attack patients aspirin and beta blockers
upon arrival, treating heart-failure patients with ACE inhibitors (drugs that
reduce the heart's workload), and giving pneumonia patients antibiotics within
four hours of arrival.

4872:	The Centers for Medicare & Medicaid Services offered hospitals a 0.4 percent
increase in reimbursement if they would report how often they met 10 of the
guidelines, and nearly all complied.

4873:	Rhode Island hospitals had gotten a head start in this effort thanks to 1998
state law, filed by Lt. Gov.

4874:	Charles J. Fogarty during his last term in the
state Senate, which required the Health Department to measure and report on the
quality of care in every health-care institution.

4875:	The department started with
hospitals.

4876:	Eventually Rhode Island became one of seven states in a pilot project
testing the procedures for collecting and reporting the data.

4877:	Hospitals in Rhode Island, as a group, ranked above the national average on each
of the 10 measures and took first place in five of them.

4878:	Rhode Island's
hospitals were weakest in meeting the guidelines calling for elderly patients to
be vaccinated against pneumonia and to receive an antibiotic within four hours.

4879:	"Rhode Island hospitals worked together collaboratively from the get-go," said
Health Director Gifford.

4880:	[The high
ranking] shows that they responded to this initiative and did a good job."

4881:	The federal government has billed Hospital Compare as a tool for consumers to
choose a hospital, but Gifford doesn't agree with that.

4882:	Such data is more useful
as a prod to get hospitals to improve.

4883:	"

The differences among hospitals aren't significant enough to justify traveling
farther for a better one, he said.

4884:	But, he said, consumers can use Hospital
Compare to become educated about the care they should receive and check with
doctors to make sure that they're getting it.

4885:	Rhode Island also shines in a different computer-based snapshot of health-care
quality, which was released Monday.

4886:	The federal Agency for Healthcare Research
and Quality ranked the states on 14 criteria, such as the percentage of adults
over 50 who receive colonoscopies and the percentage of home-health patients who
had to be hospitalized.

4887:	The state also had the
third lowest percentage of nursing home patients who were in moderate to severe
pain.

4888:	Gifford said the state's high ranking on controlling pain among nursing home
residents resulted from a concerted effort by several groups, including Brown
University and Quality Partners, to help nursing homes develop ways to diagnose
and alleviate pain.

4889:	"What you're seeing in these 14 measures," said Dr. Carolyn M. Clancy, AHRQ
director, "is shared accountability across health professions.

4890:	The buck
doesn't stop at any precisely defined point.
